==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis presents the design and development of a compact, ultra-low power geolocation
tracking device for IoT applications. The system integrates GNSS, cellular, and Wi-Fi-
based positioning into a single platform, enabling hybrid location tracking across a range
of environments. Leveraging Nordic Semiconductor’s nRF9151, nRF7000, and nPM1300
ICs, the device incorporates LPWAN connectivity, precise positioning, and power-eﬀicient
design within custom PCBs featuring wireless charging and optimized RF performance.
Custom firmware and software were developed to allow remote configuration of track-
ing method, power mode, and data visualization via a web dashboard. Field tests con-
firmed reliable connectivity, accurate positioning, and low power consumption. The results
demonstrate that high-performance geolocation can be achieved within a small form factor
without compromising energy eﬀiciency, validating the feasibility of hybrid positioning for
constrained, battery-powered systems.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications

List of Terms
Term
Description
Cellular IoT
A category of IoT technologies that
use existing cellular networks (e.g.,
LTE-M and NB-IoT) to provide se-
cure, reliable, and wide-area connec-
tivity for low-power devices.
Constrained Application Protocol
(CoAP)
A lightweight protocol designed for
resource-constrained
IoT
devices,
enabling eﬀicient RESTful commu-
nication over UDP or TCP.
Flame-Retardant 4 (FR-4)
A common PCB substrate material
known for its strength and fire resis-
tance.
Global Navigation Satellite System
(GNSS)
A satellite-based system that pro-
vides positioning, navigation, and
timing (PNT) information to re-
ceivers on Earth.
Long Term Evolution (LTE)
A standard for 4G wireless broad-
band communication that provides
high-speed data for mobile devices.
Low Power Wide Area Network
(LPWAN)
A wireless communication category
designed for long-range, low-power,
and low-data-rate IoT applications.
Includes LTE-M, NB-IoT, LoRa,
and Sigfox.
Long Term Evolution for Machines
(LTE-M)
An
LPWAN
standard
developed
for machine-to-machine (M2M) and
IoT applications within the LTE cel-
lular framework.
Narrowband IoT (NB-IoT)
Another
LPWAN
standard
de-
veloped
for
machine-to-machine
(M2M). The standard is optimized
for
low-bandwidth,
long-range,
low-power IoT devices.
nRF Connect SDK
Nordic
Semiconductor’s
software
development kit for developing ap-
plications on their nRF series of de-
vices, based on Zephyr RTOS.
Printed Circuit Board (PCB)
A board used in electronics to me-
chanically support and electrically
connect components using conduc-
tive pathways.
Power Management IC (PMIC)
An integrated circuit responsible for
managing the power requirements of
the host system by controlling volt-
age regulation, charging, and power
routing.
x
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:13==
Radio Frequency (RF)
Electromagnetic frequencies ranging
from 20 kHz to 300 GHz used in
wireless communication systems.
System-in-Package (SiP)
A packaging technology where mul-
tiple integrated circuits and passive
components are enclosed in a single
module.
Universal Asynchronous Receiver-
Transmitter (UART)
A hardware communication proto-
col that facilitates serial data com-
munication between devices.
Wi-Fi
A wireless networking technology
that enables devices to connect to
the internet or local area networks
using IEEE 802.11 standards.

Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:23==
1
Introduction
In an increasingly connected world, location awareness has become a core feature of modern
technology. From logistics and asset management to personal safety and smart mobility,
knowing the position of devices, people, or goods in real time is often crucial. At the
same time, the demand for smaller, more energy-eﬀicient, and maintenance-free devices
continues to grow, especially in Internet of Things (IoT) applications, presenting new
challenges for the development, such as long range, low data rate, low energy consumption,
and cost effectiveness[1].
The rapid expansion of IoT applications has driven the development of new wireless com-
munication protocols optimized for low power consumption, reduced data rates, and long
operating life. Traditional technologies like Wi-Fi and 4G are often too power-hungry for
battery-operated IoT devices. In response, a range of low-power wireless communication
standards has emerged, including Bluetooth Low Energy (BLE), LoRaWAN, Sigfox, LTE-
M, and NB-IoT. These protocols are tailored to different trade-offs in range, data rate,
coverage, and energy use, to meet the demand of various low-power applications[1].
Location tracking technologies have also evolved to support these low-power needs. Global
Navigation Satellite System (GNSS) has largely replaced traditional maps, allowing users
to navigate unfamiliar environments, track moving objects, and synchronize global sys-
tems. Together with internet connectivity, satellite positioning has become the foundation
for modern location-aware services used in smartphones, vehicles, logistics, and several
other applications[2].
While GNSS provides high-accuracy positioning outdoors, it is
energy-intensive and often unreliable in dense urban or indoor environments, as the GNSS
signals are weak by the time they reach Earth’s surface and can be blocked by buildings.
To improve coverage and reduce power consumption, GNSS can be supplemented with
alternative methods such as network-based or infrastructure-based positioning.
These
systems estimate location using existing communication infrastructure, such as Wi-Fi ac-
cess points, cellular towers, and Bluetooth beacons. By analyzing the characteristics of
the surrounding infrastructure, an approximate position can be determined. These tech-
nologies offer lower accuracy than GNSS but typically require much less power, and they
can operate in areas where satellite signals are unavailable[3][4].
Meanwhile, advances in semiconductor technology and battery management have enabled
the integration of these complex systems into small, power-constrained form factors, with
faster and more eﬀicient electronics. By combining eﬀicient hardware, adaptable firmware,
and intelligent power management, it is now possible to build trackers that fit in a wallet
or on a keychain, and that can operate for months or even years on a single charge.
This thesis presents the design and development of a compact, ultra-low power track-
ing device that combines GNSS, with infrastructure-based and network-based positioning
into a single platform. The system includes an onboard lithium battery, wireless charging,
and, as proof of concept, supports basic firmware for switching between different track-
ing modes. We will examine the trade-offs between power consumption and positioning
accuracy of varying tracking modes, and allow the device to adapt to various use cases.
The following chapters of this thesis detail the development process of the ultra-low power
geolocation tracker. Chapter 2 introduces the background and requirements, including rel-
evant use cases, and our proposed solution. Chapter 3 presents the theoretical foundation
used for designing and implementing our compact ultra-low power geolocation tracker.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
1
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 4 focuses on the design and fabrication of the system, including hardware se-
lection, PCB design, firmware architecture, and wireless charging integration. Chapter 5
details our measurements and results, providing validation of our hardware, performance
evaluations of the tracking technologies, and power consumption. Chapter 6 review our
system requirements and reflection of our technical decisions, and future work. Finally,
Chapter 7 concludes the thesis by summarizing the outcomes and contributions of the
project.

2
Background and Requirements
This chapter presents the background and requirements that guided the development of
our ultra-low power geolocation tracker. It begins by outlining relevant application areas
and the motivation for our design. We then review related commercial products to identify
existing capabilities and limitations. Based on this, we define the core system requirements
of our project. Finally, we present the key design decisions that shaped the development
of our solution.
2.1
Use Cases and Applications
Tracking devices are commonly used to monitor the location of assets, vehicles, and indi-
viduals across a wide range of industries, including logistics, agriculture, healthcare, and
personal safety. Many of these applications demand compact, battery-powered devices
that can operate autonomously for months or even years[5].
Traditional tracking solutions often rely solely on GNSS (Global Navigation Satellite Sys-
tems), which offers high positioning accuracy in outdoor environments. However, GNSS
has significant limitations in terms of power consumption and indoor coverage, making it
less suitable for ultra-low power IoT devices or use cases requiring reliable performance in
dense urban or indoor environments[4].
To overcome these challenges, modern tracking solutions can use hybrid approaches that
combine multiple positioning technologies, such as combining GNSS with Wi-Fi, Blue-
tooth, or other network/infrastructure-based methods. These hybrid systems enable a
trade-off between accuracy, coverage, and power eﬀiciency, allowing devices to adaptively
select the most suitable technology depending on the context and energy constraints. This
is particularly relevant for IoT deployments where devices must balance precise positioning
with long battery life[3].
For instance, Wi-Fi positioning works well in dense urban or indoor areas, where lots of
access points help pinpoint the location. Another example is cellular-based positioning,
while less precise, has the advantage of working almost anywhere there is cellular cover-
age, even in remote or underground areas where GNSS or Wi-Fi signals might be weak
or unavailable, making it an effective fallback method or useful tool for coarse geoloca-
tion. In parallel with advancements in positioning methods, low-power wide-area network
(LPWAN) technologies have emerged to support the unique needs of battery-driven IoT
devices. These protocols enable long-range, energy-eﬀicient data transmission, allowing
updates from devices to be sent without the energy overhead of traditional wireless sys-
tems. Together, these technologies enable a more flexible and power-eﬀicient approach to
geolocation across various environments[1].
That said, GNSS itself is also getting smarter and more power-eﬀicient. Technologies like
Assisted GNSS (A-GNSS) and Predicted GNSS (P-GNSS) help reduce the time it takes
to get a position fix (TTFF), which lowers energy usage. A-GNSS works by downloading
satellite data over the internet, so the receiver does not have to wait for slow satellite
transmissions. P-GNSS goes a step further by storing satellite orbit predictions for several
days ahead, allowing the receiver to get quick fixes even without a data connection[6].
2.1.1
Products on the Market
To better understand the current landscape of compact geolocation and tracking solutions,
we reviewed a selection of existing products on the market.
In the following, we will

Apple AirTag
The Apple AirTag is a small, coin-sized tracking device that works by sending out a
Bluetooth signal. When a supported Find My network device is nearby, it detects this
secure Bluetooth signal and then transmits the location to iCloud. Since the AirTag is
not processing anything and is relying on a low-energy Bluetooth signal, its battery life
lasts more than a year. The AirTag is dependent on Find My devices and will not work
standalone. The Apple AirTag comes at a price of $29.00 (DKK 299.00) for a 1-pack and
$99.00 (DKK 999.00) for a 4-pack1 and is depicted in Figure 2.1[7].
KeySmart SmartCard
The KeySmart SmartCard is a compact tracking card designed to fit into a wallet as seen in
Figure 2.2. It utilizes Apple’s Find My network for positioning, relying on Bluetooth Low
Energy (BLE) and the proximity of Apple devices. The device is designed with minimal
power consumption in mind and offers a sleek, credit-card sized form factor. However, its
reliance on nearby Apple devices limits global or standalone tracking capabilities. The
SmartCard comes in two variations, one with wireless charging (up to 5 months of battery
life on one charge) and a lite model without charging, but with up to 2 years of battery life.
The price is $39.99 (approx. DKK 258) for the rechargeable version and $24.99 (approx.
DKK 161) for the non-rechargeable version2[8].
Copenhagen Trackers
Copenhagen Trackers develops a few different GPS tracking solutions, one of their product
is depicted in Figure 2.3. Their devices are generally larger and more industrial in design
than the KeySmart SmartCard and AirTag mentioned above. The trackers are typically
used for vehicles, assets, or bicycles. The trackers are equipped with GPS and support
NB-IoT or LTE-M connectivity, and come in a waterproof enclosure. They offer both
real-time location updates and low-power operation, depending on the configuration, all

GNSS, Wi-Fi, and Bluetooth) make it a highly relevant benchmark for compact, multi-
mode tracking devices. The SenseCAP T1000 comes at a price of $29.99 (approx. DKK
193)5[11].
Summary and Relevance
The reviewed products span a wide range of designs, from BLE-only solutions relying on
third-party device networks to fully integrated GPS and cellular or LoRaWAN devices
with varying levels of configurability. The BLE-based card tracker offers attractive form
factors and low cost, but is dependent on external networks, which limits its global and
standalone tracking capabilities. Cellular/LoRaWAN solutions like ioTracker and CPH
Tracker offer greater independence and global positioning capability, but often come with
increased cost, complexity, and size.
By examining these solutions, we gained insight

2.2
System Requirements
As mentioned previously, this project aims to develop a working ultra-low power tracking
system. We aim to offer a compact, credit-card-sized device with multiple positioning tech-
nologies, while achieving ultra-low power consumption suitable for extended deployment
times on a single battery charge, and utilizing a low-power communication technology as
the main communication method. To guide the design and development process, we have
made a set of core requirements. These are divided into essential and optional features,
depending on their priority and feasibility within the project timeline.
2.2.1
Core System Requirements
The system must, at a minimum, fulfill the following core requirements:
• Battery life: The device must support at least 180 days of battery life on a single
charge, under typical tracking conditions.
• Form factor: The hardware should fit within a 54 mm x 86 mm footprint (credit-
card size) and with a thickness of 8 mm.
• Connectivity: Utilize low-power communication (e.g., LPWAN) for energy-eﬀicient
data transmission over long distances.
• Coverage: The system should provide reliable connectivity across most of Europe.
• Positioning technologies:
– GNSS support with suﬀicient antenna performance to provide at least 20 meters
of accuracy in open-sky environments.
– 2.4 GHz Wi-Fi scanning for positioning (5 GHz support is optional).
• Power management: The system should include a power management solution
with a rechargeable battery and charging support.
6
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:29==
• Data platform: A simple front- and backend allowing for data visualization of
location data.
2.2.2
Optional Features
If time and resources allow, the following enhancements could be considered:
• Wireless charging: To improve ease of use while eliminating wear from physical
charging ports.
• Downlink configuration: Allowing remote reconfiguration of tracking intervals,
power modes, and other operational parameters via downlink.
• FOTA Support: Support of Firmware Over-The-Air updates (FOTA).
• A-GNSS/P-GNSS: Implement Assisted or Predicted GNSS for faster positioning
and power optimization during GNSS acquisition.
• Enclosure: Design a simple 3D printed enclosure for protection.
2.3
Our Solution
This project aims to develop an ultra-low power geolocation tracker that can operate ef-
ficiently for long periods while providing accurate and reliable location data. The core
idea involves switching between tracking technologies, depending on the situation, to op-
timize both power and performance, or give the end-user of the product freedom to choose
the desired operation mode for their specific needs. The primary objective is to design
a custom PCB and implement demonstration firmware that showcases the potential for
low-power operation and positioning. In addition, the device is also intended to serve as
a prototype that can support future development of similar systems and act as a flexible
hardware platform for others to build on and integrate their own applications.
A key design choice in this project is the use of Cellular IoT (NB-IoT and LTE-M) over
alternative low-power communication network solutions such as LoRaWAN, Sigfox, and
Zigbee. While these technologies are highly energy-eﬀicient, they present limitations in
coverage, infrastructure, and data transmission. Cellular IoT, by contrast, offers excel-
lent coverage as it uses the existing LTE networks, ensuring wide availability without the
need to implement additional infrastructure. Additionally, other low-power communica-
tion technologies are often optimized for small, infrequent data packets, whereas Cellular
IoT can handle larger payloads and more frequent updates, which can improve tracking
resolution, while still offering low power consumption[12]. This will be further explained
in Chapter 3.
Our proposed system features a custom-designed PCB that integrates the Nordic Semicon-
ductors nRF9151 SoC, offering both GNSS and LTE-M/NB-IoT capabilities into a single
module. The tracker will be using GNSS for outdoor positioning and LTE-M/NB-IoT
for cellular communication, enabling data transmission over the existing mobile networks
worldwide. To improve location accuracy indoors or in areas where GNSS is unavailable,
the system will also have Wi-Fi scanning through the nRF7000 Integrated Circuit (IC),
allowing for Wi-Fi positioning. In addition, the system can utilize cellular-based posi-
tioning as a fallback method in areas where both GNSS and Wi-Fi are unavailable. The
implementation and design of our custom PCB is explained in Chapter 4. The location
data from our device will be sent to a backend, where it is stored and made available
through a web-based front-end for visualization, while also allowing the user to configure
the device remotely.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications

2.4
Summary
We have explored relevant use cases across industries and highlighted the limitations of
traditional GNSS-only solutions and how hybrid positioning using multiple tracking meth-
ods can help ensure accuracy and eﬀiciency across various environments.
A review of
existing products on the market highlights trade-offs between power, size, coverage, and
cost. Based on these insights, we formulated a set of core system requirements and op-
tional features to guide our design. These include a credit-card-sized form factor, at least
180 days of battery life, and multi-mode positioning. Our solution will be integrating
the nRF9151 SiP and nRF7000 IC on a custom PCB, enabling both GNSS, Wi-Fi, and
cellular positioning, and LTE-M/NB-IoT connectivity.

3
Technical Background
This chapter outlines the core technologies and concepts that support the design and
implementation of our compact ultra-low power geolocation tracker. We begin by intro-
ducing the Internet of Things (IoT), and then explore various communication technologies
used in low-power IoT systems, including both short-range and long-range wireless pro-
tocols. These include Bluetooth Low Energy (BLE), Wi-Fi, LoRaWAN, Sigfox, Zigbee,
and Cellular IoT technologies such as LTE-M and NB-IoT. We will showcase the different
trade-offs for each of these protocols and why Cellular IoT is the most suitable communi-
cation technology for our use case.
Following this, we will examine several positioning technologies, GNSS, Wi-Fi, and cellular-
based positioning, while highlighting their strengths, weaknesses, and relevance to low-
power tracker devices. Subsequent sections will cover relevant RF and PCB design con-
siderations, used for optimal signal performance and hardware design. A section is also
dedicated to the custom design of wireless charging coils.
Lastly, we touch on micro-
controller architectures and firmware development approaches to provide context for the
implementation decisions presented in the subsequent chapters.
3.1
Internet of Things
The Internet of Things (IoT) refers to a network of devices–”things”– that contain em-
bedded technologies to connect or exchange data with other devices or systems over the
internet. IoT devices are therefore typically specialized hardware equipped with sensors,
actuators, software, and connectivity capabilities that enable them to interact with their
environment and communicate with other systems[13][14].
The main goal of IoT is to make systems smarter, more eﬀicient, and easier to manage.
Instead of people manually checking on things, IoT enables devices to monitor themselves,
communicate with each other, and even take action when needed. This technology spans a
wide range of applications, from consumer electronics such as wearable fitness trackers to
industrial systems used for predictive maintenance, remote monitoring, and automation.
Driven by advances in low-power computing, wireless communication, cloud infrastructure,
and machine learning, IoT has become one of the most important technologies of the 21st
century[13][15]. According to industry forecasts by IoT Analytics, the number of connected
IoT devices worldwide is expected to exceed 40 billion by 2030. This growth reflects the
increasing adoption of IoT across industries and consumer markets[16].
The basic architecture of IoT is depicted in Figure 3.1. The architecture can be broken
down into four layers: perception, network, cloud, and application layer. The perception
layer holds all the IoT devices with embedded sensors and actuators for interaction with
the surroundings. These devices are typically also equipped with a microcontroller (MCU)
for processing data locally and a connectivity interface (either a wired or wireless module)
for transmitting the data over the network layer. The network layer is responsible for
securely communicating the data collected from the perception layer to the cloud layer or
between devices in the perception layer. The cloud layer provides the infrastructure for
data storage, processing, and advanced analytics. Finally, the application layer delivers
the customer services and applications tailored to specific use cases, enabling meaningful
interactions and visualization of data and devices[17].
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
9
==PAGE:32==
Figure 3.1: Basic architecture of the IoT, illustrating the four layers: perception, network,
cloud, and application.
Figure 3.2: Comparison of various wireless communication technologies[19].
3.2
Communication Technologies in Low-Power IoT
Communication is fundamental to IoT applications, enabling devices to exchange data
over various distances and locations while balancing power eﬀiciency, cost, and scalability.
Depending on the use case, the level of connectivity required can vary — from real-
time, high-reliability links to infrequent, low data rate transmissions.
In 2023, Wi-Fi
(31%), Bluetooth (25%), and cellular IoT (21%) accounted for nearly 80% of all global IoT
connections, making them the most widely used connectivity technologies today[16]. Each
communication technology, along with other LPWANs (e.g., LoRaWAN, Sigfox) and mesh
protocols (e.g., Zigbee), offers unique strengths depending on application requirements
such as range, power eﬀiciency, data rate, infrastructure availability, and scalability[18].
An overview and comparison of various wireless communication technologies are shown in
Figure 3.2.
For our compact ultra-low power geolocation tracker, choosing the right communication
technology is crucial to ensuring we get a product with good battery life, reliable data
transmission, and coverage. In the following sections, we compare some of the different
communication categories and technologies within each category to determine the best
option for our use case.
10
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:33==
3.2.1
LAN/PAN
Local Area Network (LAN) and Personal Area Network (PAN) technologies are primarily
intended for short-range communication and are commonly used in indoor or spatially
constrained environments.
These technologies typically offer high data rates and low
latency but come at the cost of increased power consumption and limited coverage, making
them more suitable for IoT applications with a reliable power source.
Wi-Fi is the most widely deployed LAN technologies, with its high throughput and low
latency, making it suitable for data-intensive application such as security cameras or smart
home systems. However, as Wi-Fi is generally not optimized for power eﬀiciency, it is not
well-suited for battery-operated devices that require long operational periods, but is more
suited for applications that require high data rates and do not require extensive coverage.
Even with its widespread adoption, its scalability is limited compared to other IoT network
types[18][20].
Bluetooth, and especially its Low Energy variant (BLE), is a leading PAN solution for
short-range, energy-eﬀicient communication. BLE is well-suited for wearable devices or
short-range sensing applications. BLE also support mesh networking, which can allow
for broader coverage in local deployments. For BLE-only devices, to transfer data to the
internet, another BLE device with an IP connection is needed for receiving this data and
sending it to the internet[20][21]. A notable example is Apple’s Find My network, which
uses BLE to enable location tracking of devices like AirTags. Here the network acts as a
crowdsourced network with hundreds of millions of Apple devices, which uses Bluetooth
to locate missing devices or items nearby[22].
While Wi-Fi and Bluetooth technologies provide valuable capabilities, their limited range,
dependency on nearby infrastructure (e.g., routers or smartphones), and high power con-
sumption for Wi-Fi make them less suitable for our battery-driven remote geolocation
tracker.
Nonetheless, they can serve complementary roles as explained in Section 3.5,
with Wi-Fi scanning allowing for location estimation and as a useful tool for low-power
geolocation tracking.
3.2.2
Mesh protocols
Zigbee and Thread are both low-power, wireless mesh standards operating on the IEEE
802.15.4 standard. Both are designed for short-range, low-power communication between
many devices. Unlike point-to-point networks, mesh topologies allow each device to relay
messages for others, improving local coverage and network resilience[23]. They both oper-
ate in the 2.4 GHz ISM band (Zigbee can also support the 800 to 900 MHz band), offering
data rates up to 250 kbps and support for large networks. However both technologies have
limited range, typically 20-100 meters, and are susceptible to interference as they operate
in the 2.4 GHz band. Thread is a newer IP-based mesh protocol, supporting IPv6, devel-
oped and promoted by Google, Samsung, and several other silicon vendors who wanted a
low-power mesh IP network[24][25].
While both Zigbee and Thread are excellent for local, battery-powered device networks,
their limited range and dependency on dense device networks make them unsuitable for
wide-area geolocation tracking applications.
3.2.3
LPWAN
Low Power Wide Area Networks (LPWAN) technologies are specifically designed for low-
bandwidth, long-range communication with minimal power consumption, which makes
them well-suited for battery-driven IoT devices that need to transmit small amounts of
data every now and then. Various LPWAN technologies have arisen in the unlicensed
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
11
==PAGE:34==
(a) Helium coverage in Denmark.
(b) Cibicom coverage in Denmark.
(c) TTN coverage in Denmark.
Figure 3.3: Helium, Cibicom, and TTN LoRaWAN network coverage in Denmark from
their respective coverage maps.
and licensed frequency bands, where the most prominent technologies include LoRaWAN,
Sigfox, and cellular-based options such as LTE-M and NB-IoT. Each of these solutions
presents trade-offs in terms of data rate, coverage, power consumption, cost, and scala-
bility. In the following we have categorized the technologies into two groups: non-cellular
LPWAN and cellular LPWAN[1][26].
3.2.4
Non-Cellular LPWAN
Non-cellular LPWAN technologies provide long-range, low-power connectivity over unli-
censed spectrum, making them well-suited for IoT applications with low data requirements.
The leading technologies in this category are LoRaWAN and Sigfox.
LoRaWAN (Long Range Wide Area Network) is a low-power, long-range wireless proto-
col designed for IoT devices. LoRAWAN uses LoRa modulation, a technique derived from
Chirp Spread Spectrum (CSS) technology. Here it encodes the information on radio waves
using chirp pulses, which make the LoRa modulated signal robust against disturbance and
can be received across great distances. It operates in unlicensed frequency sub-gigahertz
bands[27]. LoRaWAN’s modulation and low data rate make it particularly effective for
applications requiring long-range communication with minimal power consumption.
Despite its long range and low power consumption, LoRaWAN is not suitable for real-time
applications due to its low data rate and high latency. Additionally, it requires gateway
infrastructure to forward data from end devices to the internet, which adds complexity to
12
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:35==
deployment and limits connectivity for regions without gateways[24]. This infrastructure
is available through providers like The Things Network (TTN), Helium, Cibicom (in Den-
mark), or can be privately deployed. Unfortunately, the availability and density of such
gateways significantly impact coverage and reliability of the network. Some of the cover-
age maps of Denmark is shown in Figure 3.3, highlighting the inconsistency of gateways.
For this reason, we find LoRaWAN incompatible with the requirements of our geolocation
tracker, due to the limited and unpredictable coverage.
Sigfox is another LPWAN technology designed for ultra-low power and low-bandwidth
IoT applications. Like LoRaWAN, it operates in unlicensed ISM bands, for example, 868
MHz in Europe, 915 MHz in North America, and 433 MHz in Asia. Sigfox uses ultra-
narrowband signals and focuses on minimal message transmissions rather than continuous
data streaming. Sigfox allows a maximum payload length of 12 bytes for uplink and 8
bytes for downlink, while only allowing 140 uplink messages and 4 downlink messages
per day, and a maximum throughput of only 100 bps. This gives Sigfox excellent range
and battery life, with antennas having ranges of up to 10 km in urban areas and 40 km in
rural areas, but its strict data and message limits make it unsuitable for use cases requiring
frequent or large data transmissions[1].
While Sigfox offers good benefits in terms of power consumption and long-range communi-
cation, it has faces challenges related to long-term viability and financial problems. Sigfox
follows a single-operator model, allowing only one network operator per country[28]. This
means if the operator shuts down, the entire network in that country goes offline, as seen
in Denmark, where the national operator IoT Denmark went bankrupt in 2022, and the
network was at risk of being shut down. Fortunately, Heliot Europe has taken over the
network and support for Sigfox in Denmark[29]. Globally, the founding company Sigfox,
also filed for bankruptcy in early 2022 due to financial diﬀiculties. Although the assets
were acquired by the company UnaBiz, this showcases the financial setbacks Sigfox has
have during it lifetime[30][31].
That said, coverage is still quite good in most of Europe, and networks are under roll-out
for supporting 70+ countries[32]. But despite the promising coverage, Sigfox is not a viable
option for our geolocation tracker. The strict limitations on daily uplink and downlink
messages, combined with the small payloads, will restrict the tracker’s ability to support
multiple positioning technologies or frequent location updates. Furthermore, the reliance
on a single network operator per country introduces a significant risk to long-term service
availability.
3.2.5
Cellular LPWAN (LTE-M / NB-IoT)
Traditional cellular networks have been designed to accommodate human-centric com-
munication, being optimized for high data rates and low latency, and the demand for
higher-quality voice communication and faster internet speeds has historically driven the
evolution of cellular technologies. However, the rapid expansion of IoT over the last few
decades has given a new category of applications, which has given cellular technologies a
new direction for growth. As a result, LTE Cat M1 and LTE Cat NB1, also known as
LTE-M and NB-IoT, were introduced [33]. Table 3.1 gives a brief and simplified overview
of the cellular technologies and their releases.

LTE-M
Long Term Evolution for Machines (LTE-M, also known as LTE Cat-M1), is an LPWAN
technology introduced on 3GPP’s Release 13 in June 2016. The technology is designed
for IoT connectivity, enabling a wide range of IoT devices to stay connected while trans-
mitting data eﬀiciently and reliably. LTE-M is adapted from LTE, meaning LTE-M can
be considered a subset of LTE, which also means that the two technologies share many
similarities in network architecture and protocols. The technology has also inherited many
of the security features from LTE. LTE-M operates within the licensed LTE spectrum and
is compatible with existing LTE infrastructure, allowing network operators to deploy it
through software upgrades rather than requiring entirely new hardware[38].
LTE-M operates with a bandwidth of 1.4 MHz, with uplink and downlink speeds of 300 and
375 kbps, respectively. This is among the highest in LPWANs and allow LTE-M to support
rather heavy downlink and uplink applications. Additionally, LTE-M offers low latency,
typically in the range of tens of milliseconds[33]. Just as for regular LTE, LTE-M supports
both roaming and handover, enabling devices to move between countries and maintain
seamless connectivity while moving between cell towers. This makes LTE-M particularly
suitable for mobile IoT applications such as vehicle tracking or asset monitoring[38].
NB-IoT
Narrowband Internet of Things (NB-IoT, also known as ”LTE Cat-NB1” and ”LTE Cat-
NB2”) is an LPWAN technology based on narrowband radio technology. NB-IoT (LTE
Cat-NB1) was published and standardized in Release 13 of the 3GPP in June 2016, and
was specifically designed for IoT, enabling good coverage, low-cost connectivity, low power
consumption, and support for a large number of devices[39][40]. Its successor, LTE Cat-
NB2, introduced in 3GPP Release 14 in June 2017, improved some of the feature of the first
release with better data rates, lower latency, and enhanced positioning capabilities[41].
NB-IoT operates in licensed spectrum and can co-exist with the existing LTE and GSM
infrastructure, meaning NB-IoT can be supported with the already existing cellular infras-
tructure. It supports a narrow bandwidth of 180 kHz and can be deployed in three modes:
in-band (within an LTE carrier), guard-band (using unused resource blocks within LTE),
or standalone (on a dedicated GSM spectrum)[39]. The narrow bandwidth also means
that NB-IoT focuses its power in a narrower band, accepting the decrease in data rate,
1The maximum data rates in this table are based on the sources cited. The actual maximum data rates
varies depending on the source and specific variant or update of the standards.
2Later increased to 171 kbps with GPRS (”2.5G”) and 384 kbps with EDGE (”2.75G”).
3Later increased to 7.2 Mbps with HSDPA (”3.5G”).
4Often marketed as 4G, ordinary LTE does not meet the requirements of 4G, so technically it is (”3.9G”).

Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:37==
but gains an increase in coverage and range. Therefore, NB-IoT is also more suitable for
applications requiring deep penetration or wide coverage areas[33].
As NB-IoT is inherent from traditional cellular networks, it means it also inherits the
security features, like device authentication, data encryption, and secure communication
protocols5[41].
Power Saving Techniques
Figure 3.4: Example of a PSM cycle showing transitions between PSM, RRC Connected,
and RRC Idle modes[42].
Both LTE-M and NB-IoT offer support for the power saving techniques Power Saving Mode
(PSM) and extended Discontinuous Reception (eDRX) to minimize power consumption.
PSM enables devices to minimize their power consumption to the bare minimum while
remaining connected to the network. The devices enter sleep mode and will no longer
monitor paging, making them unreachable. For the devices to keep their connectivity
status, they will need to wake up regularly and send a Tracking Area Update (TAU) to
the network. The period between wake ups is decided by the Periodic TAU timer, which
is provided by the operator if the network supports PSM[42].
An example is showcased in Figure 3.4. Upon waking, the device enters RRC6 Connected
mode to send and receive data. Once the RRC Inactivity Timer expires, it transitions to
RRC Idle, where it briefly listens for downlink data during the Active Timer period. If no
further data is exchanged, the device returns to PSM[42].
eDRX is extending the concept of standard DRX used in LTE networks. DRX allows
connected devices to enter sleep mode between periodic wakeups to check for incoming
data. The period is typically a few seconds and therefore not optimized for low-power IoT
applications. eDRX is increasing the sleep duration between wakeups, thereby conserving
energy. Connected devices can request the duration of both the eDRX cycle length (the
5It is still recommended to implement additional security measures to mitigate potential vulnerabilities.
6RRC (Radio Resource Control) is a protocol that manages the connection state between the device and
the cellular network, controlling when the device is actively connected (RRC Connected) or in a low-power
idle state (RRC Idle).
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
15
==PAGE:38==
Figure 3.5: Two examples of eDRX connection states, left showing idle paging cycles, right
includes data transfer and transition through RRC Connected and Inactivity timer before
returning to sleep[42].
interval between listening windows) and the paging time window (when the device is
awake and listening for messages) from the network. Just as for PSM, connected devices
are also unreachable during sleep, meaning longer eDRX cycles lead to increase latency
for incoming communication to the devices[42]. Two examples are shown in Figure 3.5.
On the left, the connected device is waking up to monitor pages without any incoming
data. On the right, the device wakes up to a page that requires a data transfer and then
return to sleep until the next eDRX cycle.
An important note, although we have described PSM and eDRX as two separate power-
saving techniques, they can be used together, and optimize different phases of the connec-
tion cycle.

A direct comparison of the two cellular LPWANs is shown in Table 3.2. Both LTE-M and
NB-IoT offer good coverage and are supported in many countries worldwide, as shown in
Figure 3.6. Unfortunately, unlike LTE-M, NB-IoT does not really support roaming. LTE-
M has the advantage of having inherited the cellular global roaming agreements, meaning
16
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:39==
Figure 3.6: Mobile IoT deployment map showing coverage of LTE-M and NB-IoT[43].
NB-IoT is far behind, making roaming quite limited, and depending on the area of interest
and operator[44].
Another drawback of NB-IoT is when it comes to maintaining reliable connectivity while
the device is in motion, and supporting seamless handovers between cell towers. LTE-M
has a clear advantage in both areas. It natively supports handovers, allowing a device
to transition between coverage areas without losing connection, making it ideal for mo-
bile applications such as asset tracking or wearables. In contrast, NB-IoT initially did
not support handovers, meaning a device would need to reattach to a new cell tower
after leaving the range of the original, which increases latency and power consumption.
However, this limitation was partially addressed with the introduction of LTE Cat-NB2,
with improved mobility features. Still, LTE-M remains more suitable for applications that
require support for mobility[33][44].
Unlike Sigfox and LoRaWAN, which are employed in unlicensed spectra and have asyn-
chronous communication protocols, making them more susceptible to various forms of
interference, NB-IoT and LTE-M are both employed in a licensed spectrum and use an
LTE-based synchronous protocol. This gives NB-IoT and LTE-M a guaranteed good qual-
ity of service (QoS)[39].
In terms of power consumption, LTE-M is generally more eﬀicient because its higher data
rate and lower latency allow data to be transmitted more quickly, reducing the time the
radio needs to stay active. In contrast, NB-IoT requires more time to transmit the same
data and also takes longer during the attach procedure to the cell towers[33].
Finally, Freedom to leave, which refers to the ability to change mobile network operators
remotely by updating the SIM profile over the air. LTE-M generally supports this ca-
pability, where NB-IoT typically lacks this support, which can make operator switching
diﬀicult once devices are deployed[44].
In summary, both LTE-M and NB-IoT offer some advantages over other low-power com-
munication technologies we have discussed for our use case. However, LTE-M is ultimately
better suited for our geolocation tracker. Its excellent support for mobility, lower latency,
global roaming capabilities, and compatibility with remote SIM provisioning make it a
more robust and flexible option—particularly for a device expected to operate across dif-
ferent regions and potentially while in motion. These features make LTE-M the optimal
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
17
==PAGE:40==
Figure 3.7: LTE-M and NB-IoT network coverage in Denmark[45].
choice for delivering reliable connectivity in our compact, ultra-low power tracking appli-
cation.
3.3
Communication Protocols for IoT (HTTP, MQTT,
CoAP, LwM2M)
Besides utilizing low-power communication networks to minimize power consumption for
battery-driven devices, another aspect is the choice of communication protocol.
This
section compares four commonly used protocols: HTTP, MQTT, CoAP, and LwM2M,
with a focus on power eﬀiciency, protocol overhead, and suitability for low-duty-cycle
operation.
Hypertext Transfer Protocol (HTTP) is a widely used request-response protocol designed
for the web.
While it is simple and well-supported, it is not optimized for low-power
IoT devices due to its high overhead, persistent connections, and large message size. The
requirement for frequent handshakes and the use of TCP makes it energy ineﬀicient. Given
these limitations, HTTP is not ideal for our tracking system, where power eﬀiciency is a
key concern[46].
Constrained Application Protocol (CoAP) is a lightweight, UDP-based protocol specifically
designed for low-power IoT devices. It follows a similar structure to HTTP, making it easy
to integrate with existing web technologies, but with significantly lower overhead. Unlike
HTTP, CoAP operates over UDP, reducing connection overhead and improving energy
eﬀiciency. Additionally, CoAP supports asynchronous communication, allowing the device
to enter low-power modes between transmissions. These features could make CoAP the
preferred choice for our tracking device, as it is optimized for periodic connectivity for
networks, like NB-IoT and LTE-M, while minimizing power consumption[47].
Message Queuing Telemetry Transport (MQTT) is a publish-subscribe protocol designed
for low-bandwidth IoT applications. It enables asynchronous communication, where de-
vices publish messages to a broker, and subscribers receive updates only when new data
is available. However, MQTT requires a persistent TCP connection to the broker, which
can increase power consumption for battery-operated devices. While MQTT is eﬀicient
for continuous, real-time messaging, it is less suited for applications with infrequent trans-
missions, as maintaining an active connection consumes additional energy.
To reduce
18
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:41==
power consumption, MQTT can be used with long keep-alive intervals, but this comes
with trade-offs in latency and responsiveness[46].
Lightweight Machine to Machine (LwM2M) is a device management protocol built on
top of CoAP. It is optimized for low-power operation and provides features for remote
device management, such as firmware updates, configuration, and diagnostics, which are
beneficial in large-scale IoT deployments. While LwM2M is optimized for constrained
devices and supports low-power operation, its feature set is more than our current system
requires as a proof-of-concept. Since LwM2M builds on CoAP, it is practical to start with
CoAP for eﬀicient geolocation data transmission and later integrate LwM2M if advanced
device management is needed[48][49].
In summary, CoAP seems to be the best-suited protocol for our low-power geolocation
tracker, offering minimal overhead and asynchronous communication. HTTP and MQTT
introduce unnecessary energy costs due to their reliance on TCP and connection persis-
tence, while LwM2M offers a valuable upgrade path if remote device management becomes
relevant in the future.
3.4
GNSS and Assisted GNSS Techniques
Figure 3.8: GNSS trilateration using signals from multiple satellites to determine receiver
position[50].
Global Navigation Satellite Systems (GNSS) is a constellation of Earth-orbiting satellites
with very specific trajectories. These satellites broadcast orbit information and accurate
timing (and other services) to radio receivers (GNSS receivers), which are designed to
receive those satellite signals and decode the messages from the signal. These messages
can be used to obtain the receiver’s position, also known as a GNSS fix or position fix.
As the name states, GNSS offers global coverage, ensuring that devices can determine
their position anywhere on Earth. Some of the most famous GNSS constellations are GPS
(United States), GLONASS (Russia), BeiDou (China), and Galileo (European Union).
While each GNSS system functions independently, most modern GNSS receivers often
support multiple constellations to improve accuracy and enhance performance compared
to receivers only using one GNSS constellation[51].
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
19
==PAGE:42==
Condition

Unknown
Unknown

Known
Known
Within 100 km of last fix
10 min precision
22 sec
Table 3.3: Time to First Fix (TTFF) under different GNSS start conditions[6].
To obtain a GNSS fix, the receiver must determine four unknowns: latitude, longitude,
altitude, and clock bias. Thus, the receiver must track a minimum of four satellites to
acquire a fix, as seen in Figure 3.8. The GNSS receiver uses trilateration to calculate
its position on Earth, by using the distance to the different satellites in sight, and the
accuracy of the fix can typically within 5 m[6].
Besides calculating the location solution once the receiver has at least 4 satellites, the
solution will also need to be validated as a valid fix. To calculate a valid fix, the GNSS re-
ceiver also needs information about the satellite, known as the ephemeris and the almanac.
The ephemeris is unique data for each satellite and contains precise information about ac-
curacy, health, age of data, satellite clock correction coeﬀicients, and orbital parameters.
This data is highly accurate but only valid for a few hours and must be frequently updated.
In contrast, the almanac data provides less precise but long-term orbital information for
all satellites, which means it can be valid for up to 90 days. This means both datasets
helps the receiver locate satellites more quickly when starting up. The availability of these
datasets significantly influences the Time-to-First Fix (TTFF), the duration required for
a GNSS receiver to establish an initial position fix after being powered on[6]. TTFF is
usually divided into three main categories: cold start, warm start, and hot start. The
TTFF for the three categories is depicted in Table 3.3. This makes GNSS receivers very
power-hungry, because they require the receiver to be continuously active and listening
to weak satellite signals for an extended period to download the data and compute an
accurate fix.
Moreover, GNSS accuracy and availability are highly dependent on having a clear line
of sight to multiple satellites.
This makes GNSS unsuitable for environments such as
indoor spaces, underground areas, or heavily built-up areas where satellite visibility can
be obstructed.
3.4.1
Enhancements for Faster and More Eﬀicient GNSS Fixings
Due to the continuous signal processing required for GNSS positioning, power consump-
tion is a major concern for battery-operated devices using GNSS. Several optimization
techniques have been developed to improve both TTFF and energy eﬀiciency. Assisted
GNSS (A-GNSS) accelerates fix acquisition by retrieving ephemeris and almanac data via
a communication network, e.g., cellular, reducing TTFF to less than five seconds while
also lowering power usage.
Predicted GNSS (P-GNSS) further optimizes eﬀiciency by
preloading satellite data for up to two weeks, reducing the need for frequent satellite up-
dates. Additionally, low accuracy mode allows GNSS receivers to obtain a fix with only
three satellites by using a reference altitude, minimizing power consumption at the cost
of reduced accuracy[6].
3.5
Wi-Fi Communication and Positioning
Wi-Fi communication and positioning offer an effective alternative to GNSS for location
tracking, especially in urban and indoor environments. This section examines the Wi-Fi
standards relevant to positioning and explains how access point scanning can be utilized
to estimate device location.

3.5.1
Wi-Fi Standards
IEEE 802.11 defines the protocols for Wireless Local Area Network (WLAN). Depending
on the version of the IEEE 802.11, the Wi-Fi network can use different frequencies, as
seen in Table 3.4.
The different Wi-Fi bands are divided into several channels. Depending on local regula-
tions, some of these channels may not be permitted to use as the frequencies are restricted.
In the 2.4 GHz band, channels 1 through 13 are allowed by European Telecommunications
Standards Institute (ETSI) and Japan standards, with the Federal Communications Com-
mission (FCC) only allowing channels 1-11 and Japan including channel 14. The channels
are illustrated across the 2.4 GHz band in Figure 3.10.
For the 5 GHz band, the channels are numbered from 36-64, 100-140, and 149-165, with
the channel number incrementing by 4. The 5 GHz band starts at 5180 MHz for the center
frequency of channel 36 and 5825 MHz for channel 1658[54].

In the newest Wi-Fi standards, such as Wi-Fi 6E, Wi-Fi 7, and Wi-Fi 8, a new 6 GHz
band is included. This band has channels starting at 5935 MHz up to 7115 MHz. These
standards (Wi-Fi 6E and Wi-Fi 7) are still in their early days and have not yet been as
widely adopted as their older counterparts on 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz.
Based on our analysis of data from WIGLE.net[52], the top 25 Wi-Fi channels—shown
in Figure 3.11—account for approximately 90% of all scanned channels. All of these top
channels fall within the 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz frequency bands. Furthermore, we found that
about two-thirds of the top 25 channels, corresponding to 60% of all scanned networks,
operate in the 2.4 GHz band. Given this distribution, our focus will be on the 2.4 GHz
and 5 GHz bands for Wi-Fi-based positioning, as discussed further in Section 3.5.2.
3.5.2
Wi-Fi Positioning
Wi-Fi access points broadcast two public values: the SSID (the network’s name) and
the BSSID (the MAC address of the NIC broadcasting that name). Since the early 2010s,
companies including Google—whose Street View cars cataloged nearby SSIDs and BSSIDs
worldwide—and, more recently, Apple and Microsoft via smartphones and computers have
recorded this data to enable location services[55]. WIGLE.net is another database that
relies on user-submitted data. By scanning for nearby Wi-Fi networks and comparing ob-
served BSSIDs weighted by signal strength against such databases, the geographic location
of the device can be found[52]. An example of this process can be seen in Figure 3.12.
Wi-Fi positioning achieves moderate accuracy, typically 10 to 30 meters, and is more
power-eﬀicient than GNSS, while also working indoors[3][56]. However, a key limitation is
that the accuracy and availability of a fix depend entirely on the presence of the scanned
access points in the database. If the Wi-Fi networks in the area are not indexed or no
access points are present, no position fix can be made.
3.6
Cellular Positioning
In addition to enabling wireless communication for the device, cellular network connections
can be used to estimate a device’s geographic location based on nearby cell towers. The
method can serve as an alternative when satellite-based positioning, such as GNSS, is
unavailable, unreliable, or too power-intensive.
22
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:45==
Figure 3.11: An excerpt of channel statistics from the WIGLE.net database[52].

23
==PAGE:46==
Figure 3.12: Illustration of Wi-Fi positioning: the device scans for nearby Wi-Fi access
points and sends the access point information (MAC addresses and signal strengths) to a
cloud service, which estimates the device’s location using a Wi-Fi access point database.
Figure 3.13: Illustration of cellular positioning: the device collects information about

Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications

The device collects details about surrounding cell towers, information typically obtained
during network connection, and sends it to a server or cloud service. Using databases like
Unwired Labs or Google’s Geolocation API, the server estimates the device’s location and
relays it back to the user or device, as illustrated in Figure 3.13. Although less accurate
than GNSS or Wi-Fi positioning, cellular-based location fixes are more power-eﬀicient,
particularly if the device is already connected to the cellular network. Accuracy generally
ranges around one kilometer, but can improve to a few hundred meters if information from
multiple cell towers is used[57][58].
Like Wi-Fi positioning, it depends on cloud-based databases containing indexed cellular
towers, and a fix cannot be made if the relevant tower information is missing.
3.7
RF Design Considerations
Wireless communication systems depend on the eﬀicient transmission and reception of
radio frequency (RF) signals. Understanding transmission lines and impedance matching
is fundamental to minimizing signal loss and ensuring reliable performance.
Without
proper matching, issues like reflections can degrade system functionality[59]. This section
introduces some core RF concepts relevant to PCB design, including transmission lines,
impedance matching, and common layout techniques such as microstrip and coplanar
waveguides. It will also present critical metrics like return loss, reflection coeﬀicient, and
VSWR, which are used to evaluate RF performance.
3.7.1
Transmission Lines
For RF systems, transmission lines are used to carry high-frequency signals between com-
ponents such as antennas, filters, amplifiers, and transceivers. RF transmission lines are
designed with controlled impedance, typically 50 Ω. This is to match the impedance of
other parts of the RF system, as 50 Ωis a common standard for RF systems. The basic idea
of impedance matching is to reduce reflection and preserve signal integrity. Impedance
mismatch will cause power to be reflected back to its source from the impedance mismatch
boundary, which will create standing waves and cause power to be lost in the transmis-
sion lines instead of being transmitted to the load or other parts of the system. This can
potentially cause malfunction of the RF circuit[59].
There is many types of transmission lines. For PCB design, the most common types are
the microstrip and coplanar waveguides. The geometry of the trace and the surrounding
ground and dielectric materials defines the impedance of each of these transmission lines.
The reason to use transmission lines in your PCB design and not just simple copper traces
is that when dealing with high-frequency signals, such as RF signals, the wavelength of the
signals becomes comparable to the length of the PCB traces, and the trace behaves as a
transmission line rather than a simple conductor. Unlike when working with low-frequency
signals, where the trace length is much shorter than the signal wavelength, making the
transmission line effect negligible, and impedance matching is typically unnecessary for
these signals[59].
3.7.2
Microstrip Lines and Coplanar Waveguides
Microstrip consists of a signal trace on the outer PCB layer above a continuous ground
plane, as depicted in Figure 3.14.
It is widely used due to its simplicity, accessibility
for components and probing/measurements (compared to embedded PCB transmission
lines), and ease of fabrication. Some of the disadvantages of the microstrip are that the
electromagnetic field is not completely confined, making it more susceptible to radiation
and coupling from nearby signals. Also, once you have selected your dielectric thickness,
the other dimensions of your trace are determined, which can be inconvenient when con-
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
25
==PAGE:48==
Figure 3.14: Microstrip transmission line structure with a signal trace above a continuous
ground plane[60].
Figure 3.15: Coplanar waveguide structures, first with signal and ground traces on the
same layer (CPW), secondly with additional ground plane (CPWG), and last with ground
plane and vias (GCPW)[62].
necting to other components, if the dimensions between the trace and the footprint do not
match[59][61].
Coplanar Waveguides tries to address some of the disadvantages of the microstrip. The
coplanar waveguide is placing both the signal and ground conductors on the same layer
(CPW) or as a coplanar waveguide with ground (CPWG) or with both ground plane and
ground vias, also known as grounded coplanar waveguide (GCPW), as seen in Figure 3.15.
This geometry allows for control of the impedance by adjusting the trace width and/or
the ground gap, making the coplanar waveguide ideal for routing impedance-controlled
connections to fine-pitch components.
For multilayer designs, the coplanar waveguide
with an additional ground plane and ground vias, are typical configurations[61]. These
configurations provide additional shielding due to the surrounding grounded copper pour,
ground plan, and vias[63].
The characteristic impedance for both microstrip and coplanar waveguides depends on
26
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:49==
factors like trace width, dielectric height, and spacing to ground. The approximate formu-
las for these calculation are beyond the scope of this thesis, and these values are typically
calculated using dedicated impedance calculator software rather than analytical equations,
as real-world PCB stackups are more complex than approximate formulas typically can
handle[59].
3.7.3
Reflection Coeﬀicient, VSWR, and Matching Networks
While controlled impedance transmission lines such as microstrip and coplanar waveguides
are essential for preserving signal integrity across a PCB, they do not by themselves ensure
that energy is eﬀiciently transferred between interconnected components. In RF systems,
any difference between the source and load impedances can result in signal reflections,
standing waves, and power loss.
When two components have mismatched impedances, some of the signal is reflected back
toward the source rather than being delivered to the load, resulting in a loss of power,
but it can also decrease the signal-to-noise ratio of the signal. To address this, designers
employ impedance matching networks, which transform the impedance of a load or source
to match the characteristic impedance of the system, typically 50 Ω. These matching
networks can take various forms, but one of the simplest types is the L-network, made of
two reactive components, designed to arbitrary load impedances from components to the
characteristic impedance of the transmission line[59].
When a signal encounters an impedance mismatch, a portion of its power is reflected.
The severity of this mismatch is described by the reflection coeﬀicient, Γ, or the VSWR
(Voltage Standing Wave Ratio). The reflection coeﬀicient is defined as
Γ = ZL −Z0
ZL + Z0
[59].
(3.1)
where ZL is the load impedance (e.g., the antenna), and Z0 is the source or characteristic
impedance of the transmission line. For the maximum power to be delivered to the load,
a Γ = 0 is required, indicating no reflected wave. This is obtained when the impedance
of the load ZL and the characteristic impedance Z0 is equal, which also implies that the
line and load are matched. For impedance mismatch, the loss of power is defined as the
Return Loss and is defined as
RL = −20 log |Γ| dB [59].
(3.2)
When line and load are matched the return loss is ∞dB, and 0 dB if there is total
reflection (all incident power is reflected, |Γ| = 1) [59].
For example, a return loss of
20 dB corresponds to 1% of power being reflected, while 10 dB corresponds to 10% re-
flected. Typically, for many datasheets and simulations, the parameter S11 is representing
a negative return loss
S11 = −RL = 20 log |Γ| dB [64].
(3.3)
Another related parameter is the VSWR, which also describes the quality of the impedance
mismatch by the ratio of the maximum and minimum voltage amplitudes on the trans-
mission line
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
27
==PAGE:50==
VSWR = 1 + |Γ|
1 −|Γ| [59].
(3.4)
A perfect impedance match will have a VSWR = 1, and an impedance mismatch is
indicated with an VSWR > 1.
3.8
PCB Design Fundamentals
Figure 3.16: PCB stackup example with 8 layers[65].
Printed Circuit Board (PCB) design is a foundational discipline in modern electronics,
and involves the physical realization of electrical devices. PCBs provides the necessary
connections and support for various component, making them the backbone of nearly any
electronic product. A well-designed PCB not only connects components electrically by
routing traces, but also ensures signal integrity, thermal performance, manufacturability,
and the overall functionality and reliability of the circuit. This section will outline some
of the fundamentals of PCB design and layout, that we have used during our design and
fabrication process of our custom board.
A typical PCB consists of copper layers separated by insulating materials, with traces
routing signals and vias connecting different layers, as seen in Figure 3.16. Simple designs
typically use two layers, while complex designs often require way more layers to accommo-
date good power, ground, and signal routing. Proper grounding and power distribution
are important for maintaining signal integrity and minimizing ElectroMagnetic Interfer-
ence (EMI). Using ground/power planes and decoupling capacitors can help reduce noise
and ensure stable power delivery to components[66].
Good trace routing and geometry are also essential for signal integrity, minimizing crosstalk,
and EMI. E.g., by maintaining proper spacing and using ground traces or planes as iso-
lation barriers. In multilayer PCBs, signal integrity can be preserved by separating dif-
ferent types of signals (e.g., digital, analog, RF) onto different layers and ensuring proper
shielding and spacing[66]. For high-speed signals or RF applications, proper impedance
matching and termination are needed for good power delivery and signal integrity. E.g.,
as explained in Section 3.7, RF applications need to be routed with transmission lines
with controlled impedance.
Component placement is often the starting point of the layout process. Proper placement
should reduce the distance between related components, simplifying routing and help
overall circuit performance. Another critical aspect of component placement is thermal
management. Components that dissipate significant power, such as power regulators, RF
28
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:51==
amplifiers, or processors, should be placed to allow suﬀicient heat dissipation, e.g., with
thermal vias and copper pours to spread and dissipate heat[66].
Design for manufacturability (DFM) is also a key consideration. Designers must ensure
that trace widths, clearances, via sizes, pad geometries, etc., fit within the fabrication ca-
pabilities of the chosen PCB manufacturer to ensure reliable fabrication of your device[66].
In conclusion, PCB design is a multidisciplinary process. For our project, applying these
core principles is essential to ensure a compact and reliable design.
3.9
Serial Communication
Serial communication refers to the process of sending data one bit at a time, sequentially
over a communication channel. This section will go over the three main serial communica-
tion protocols our system relies on: Inter-Integrated Circuit (I2C), Universal Asynchronous
Receiver/Transmitter (UART) and Serial Peripheral Interface (SPI).
3.9.1
Inter-Integrated Circuit (I2C)
I2C is a synchronous serial communication protocol operating in a master-slave configu-
ration. It is ideal for low-speed peripherals and allows multiple devices to be connected
on the same bus. The protocol uses two lines for transmitting and receiving data: SDA
(data) and SCL (clock). Here, a master device initiates the communication and clock
signal, while slave devices respond as addressed. As the lines are open-drain, the con-
nected devices can pull the voltage low on the line, indicating a logical ”0”, or let it float
high, indicating a logical ”1”. Pull resistors are needed for both the lines to ensure the
lines return to the high state when no device is pulling them low. I2C is widely used in
application with sensors, and other peripherals needing a simple and low-power protocol
for communication. The multi-device support makes it ideal for systems with multiple
sensors or other low-speed peripheral modules[67].
3.9.2
Universal Asynchronous Receiver/Transmitter (UART)
UART is a widely used, simple, and asynchronous communication protocol. UART uses
start and stop bits to mark the beginning and end of each data packet in data transmis-
sions. This means it can ensure proper synchronization without requiring a shared clock
line. The protocol used a dedicated transmitter (TX) and receiver (RX) lines for commu-
nication, and a shared ground. For transmission, it converts data bytes into a sequential
stream of bits and vice versa for receiving[67].
3.9.3
Serial Peripheral Interface (SPI)
SPI is a high-speed, full-duplex protocol typically used for devices requiring fast data
transfer. SPI therefore support simultaneous sending and receiving data, while also sup-
porting continuous data streams, unlike other protocols like UART and I2C. This also
makes the protocol ideal for use cases such as flash memory, where high-speed data trans-
fers are required. SPI works using four lines, SCLK (clock), MOSI (Master Out Slave In),
MISO (Master In Slave Out), and CS (Chip Select). MOSI and MISO are the two data
lines, SCLK is created by the master device for synchronize data transfer, while CS is
used to select the specific slave device to communicate with, making the protocol able to
support multiple slave devices[67].
3.10
Wireless Charging
Since our device is battery-powered, we wanted to support rechargeable batteries. We
explored wireless charging, not because it was the most eﬀicient solution, but because it
could offer some practical benefits. Originally, we considered making the device water-
proof, which would require avoiding external connectors. Wireless charging was one way
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
29
==PAGE:52==
Figure 3.17: Block diagram illustrating a wireless charging system[68].
to achieve that. On top of that, wireless charging coils are very thin, which fits well with
our goal of keeping a slim design. This section provides the technical background for our
wireless charging setup.
3.10.1
Qi Charging
As mentioned above, Wireless Power Transfer (WPT) might not be the most eﬀicient way
to transfer power, but it can be more convenient, as it eliminates the need for physical
connectors, reducing mechanical wear and improving device durability. We initially con-
sidered wireless charging both for potentially making a waterproof design and because
charging coils can be made extremely thin, helping to keep our device profile slim.
Qi charging operates on the principles of inductive coupling. Figure 3.17 illustrates this
process, where a DC voltage is converted to an AC voltage in the transmitter circuit,
which generates an alternating magnetic field in the transmitter coil (in the charger).
This induces an AC voltage in the receiver coil (in the device), and finally, the AC voltage
is converted to a DC voltage in the receiver circuit, which can charge your device.
Various types of coils can be used for wireless charging; the most commonly used are the
copper planar spiral coil and solenoid coil. The planar spiral coil is the most relevant
type for our use case, since these coils are flat and compact, which makes them ideal
for electronic devices with limited space like ours, while also allowing for eﬀicient energy
transfer over short distances[69].
However, while off-the-shelf Qi coils are suitable for our application, they can be relatively
expensive. To reduce costs, we explored the option of designing a custom Qi-compatible
coil using either a flex PCB or a standard PCB. Unfortunately, there is only limited
documentation and few design guidelines for Qi-compatible PCB coils, which means we
rely on general design rules for planar inductors and general guidelines for designing Qi-
compliant receiver coils.
Calculation of PCB coils for wireless charging
To both achieve eﬀicient WPT and design Qi-compliant coils, it is essential to create
coils with the appropriate inductive properties. For the custom PCB coils, we choose to
implement planar spiral inductors due to their simplicity and low profile. The physical
dimensions, such as number of turns (N), trace width (W), inner diameter (Di), and outer
diameter (Do) of the coils are shown in Figure 3.18.
A simplified expression for the inductance of a planar spiral inductor is obtained from

Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:53==
Figure 3.18: A planar circular inductor with dimensions.
electromagnetic principles by approximating the sides of the spiral as symmetrical current
sheets of equivalent current densities. The resulting expression is:
L = µ0N2Davc1

Equation 3.6. The constants ci are for spirals given as c1 = 1.00, c2 = 2.46, c3 = 0.00, c4 =
0.20 [70].
The fill factor is defined as

[71],
