3.11
Microcontroller Architectures and Features
A microcontroller is essentially a compact computer. It has memory, a CPU, peripherals,
and I/O interfaces, all built into a single IC[72]. Microcontrollers are the backbone of
embedded applications. They are used in all sorts of applications, from washing machines
to cars, to medical devices and safety devices.
With the rapid expansion of the IoT,
microcontrollers now power countless connected devices to provide functionality, remote
control of a device, statistics, or sensor data[73][74].
Advancements in semiconductor
manufacturing have enabled microcontrollers to become incredibly compact. As of 2025,
some devices are as small as 1.38 mm2 as seen in Figure 3.19[75].
As microcontrollers are becoming smaller, cheaper, more power-eﬀicient, and more ad-
vanced, they are also integrated into more circuits. They can be used to reduce the phys-
ical size and complexity of circuits by replacing parts of the circuit with a microcontroller
performing the task of several components.
As technology has evolved and it has become possible to include more functionality and
peripherals in a single chip form factor, SoCs (System on Chip), sometimes with specific
functionalities. They are often more advanced than a single MCU and can include multiple
CPUs[76].
A SiP (System in Package) combines multiple integrated circuits in a single package or
module. These devices allow for a smaller footprint, as the internal ICs can be stacked or
spaced close together. They also improve flexibility and integration due to the approach
of combining multiple parts in one package[77].
32
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:55==
3.12
Embedded Firmware Development Models
Firmware for microcontrollers can be developed using a wide range of models, each offering
trade-offs between low-level control and high-level abstraction. Choosing the right devel-
opment approach involves balancing several factors: development speed, power eﬀiciency,
hardware resource usage, maintainability, and flexibility.
At one end of the spectrum is full control over the hardware with bare-metal programming;
at the other end are high-level scripting environments like MicroPython. In between lies
something like RTOS, which can significantly reduce development complexity at the cost
of additional overhead. In the following we present some common firmware development
models used in embedded systems.
3.12.1
Bare‐Metal
Bare‐metal programming runs firmware straight on the MCU, without any OS or scheduler.
The main work lives in a continuous “super loop” (e.g. ’while(1)’), while interrupts handle
time‐critical tasks. The programmer is responsible for configuring and manipulating the
MCU’s memory‐mapped registers directly, since no driver layer exists between code and
silicon[78][73].
This approach minimizes RAM/CPU overhead and provides full control over every low‐level
operation. It is ideal for deeply resource‐constrained or safety‐critical applications. How-
ever, as project complexity increases, tasks like communication protocols and low‐power
management can become complex to implement without abstraction layers or APIs. Bare-
metal programming lacks conveniences provided by other methods, such as greater mod-
ularity, reduced complexity, and increased code reuse, but it offers optimized speed and
reduced flash memory usage.
3.12.2
Arduino-Style Frameworks
While technically, the application code running on an MCU using the Arduino framework
is also bare-metal programming, the Arduino framework adds a bootloader and driver-
s/libraries that create an abstraction layer for the programmer compared to writing code
controlling all registers. This abstraction simplifies development and accelerates proto-
typing. However, this comes at a cost of power eﬀiciency, execution speed, and boot time
due to the overhead of the bootloader. Arduinos can be programmed while bypassing the
bootloader using an In-System Programmer (ISP) instead of using the usual USB-UART
bridge, as the bootloader usually handles the programming[73][79]. Arduino-style frame-
works are ideal for rapid development, prototyping, and educational use, but may not be
optimal for battery-sensitive or performance-critical applications.
3.12.3
MicroPython
In recent years, with increased processing power in low cost MCUs, solutions such as
MicroPython have been created. MicroPython enables the programmer to write code for
MCUs using a subset of the Python programming language. This creates an even higher-
level of abstraction as the MCU will have to compile the Python code into bytecode.
While pre-compilation of Python code to bytecode can reduce runtime overhead during
boot-up, the MCU still executes bytecode through an embedded MicroPython interpreter,
introducing an abstraction layer. This means that MicroPython takes up more RAM than
the code would otherwise if written in plain C, and at the same time, may run slower.
This is the trade-off between ease of programming using Python and the complexity of
C, making MicroPython best suited for applications where developer eﬀiciency and code
clarity outweigh the need for low-level optimization[73][80][81][82].
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
33
==PAGE:56==
3.12.4
Real-Time Operating System
When programming microcontrollers, simple applications often do not require a Real-Time
Operating System (RTOS). Even moderately complex tasks can be managed using loops,
flags, and state machines. However, as system complexity grows – due to an increasing
number of peripherals, higher memory demands, multiple communication interfaces, or
more functionalities – using an RTOS becomes advantageous. An RTOS enables more
robust task management and simplifies handling of concurrent operations.
While the
added layer from an RTOS may reduce performance in highly optimized scenarios, it
improves maintainability and reliability in complex systems[73][83].
Using an RTOS can transition an application from being purely loop-based, potentially
wasting CPU resources, to eﬀicient task scheduling. This approach allows for the system to
enter low-power modes. An RTOS sometimes includes or provides integration for common
communication protocols and stacks (e.g., Wi-Fi or LTE), depending on the specific RTOS
distribution or middleware used. This also make RTOS a balanced middle ground between
low-level control and high-level abstraction, offering a development experience that is less
complex to program than bare-metal, while not sacrificing too much eﬀiciency or control.
3.13
Summary
This chapter provided a comprehensive overview of the core technologies and concepts rel-
evant to the design of our compact, ultra-low power geolocation tracking device. We have
outlined the Internet of Things (IoT) and various low-power communication technologies,
highlighting the trade-offs between power consumption, range, infrastructure, and scal-
ability, concluding that LTE-M is the best fit for our application. We have presented
the positioning technologies using GNSS, Wi-Fi, and cellular methods, each evaluated for
accuracy, power consumption, and suitability in different environments. Additionally, the
chapter covered essential hardware design topics, including RF layout strategies, PCB
design fundamentals, Serial Communication protocols, and wireless charging. Finally, it
discussed microcontroller architectures and various firmware development models.

4
System Design and Fabrication
In this chapter, we walk through how our idea was turned into actual hardware. Starting
from selecting the right components to designing and assembling our custom PCB, this
part of the project has been all about making the system physically real and functional.
We knew from the start that we wanted the device to be compact, eﬀicient, and capable
of tracking location through several technologies, all while keeping power consumption as
low as possible. This put our design under tight constraints, from layout and routing,
component placement and selection, and how subsystems will interact.
We will begin by reviewing the components we selected and explaining why they were a
suitable fit for the project. We then dive into how we designed the PCB, including how we
addressed RF paths, power management, and integrating everything into a credit card-
sized form factor. We will also describe how the board was manufactured, the challenges
encountered during assembly, and initial boot-up. Lastly, we will present the prototype
application firmware and web application for data visualization and remote configuration.

4.2
Component Selection and Justification
The selection of hardware components was a key part of our system design, ensuring that
the tracking device meets the power eﬀiciency, accuracy, and connectivity requirements we
wanted for long-term geolocation tracking. Our component study concentrated on ultra-
low power components optimized for LTE-M/NB-IoT connectivity, GNSS positioning,
Wi-Fi positioning, and eﬀicient power management.
4.2.1
nRF9151
We have selected the nRF9151 from Nordic Semiconductor for the LTE-M/NB-IoT and
GNSS module. The module uses the Arm Cortex-M33 @ 64MHz for the user application,
and has 1MB of flash, 256kB of RAM. We have evaluated several alternatives, but the
key reasoning for selecting the nRF9151 over competing solutions has been the follow-
ing[84][85]:
• Support for both NB-IoT/LTE-M and GNSS: Unlike some competitors that require
a separate GNSS module, the nRF9151 offers both support for GNSS and NB-
IoT/LTE-M, reducing PCB size and complexity. As the module supports both NB-
IoT and LTE-M, we are also able to make real-world testing and comparison of the
two technologies, while supporting both technologies also increases the coverage of
the device.
• Ultra-Low Power Consumption: The nRF9151 is designed for battery-powered ap-
plications, supporting low-power solutions and optimization.
• Compact SiP Design: Compared to traditional module-based solutions, the System-
in-Package (SiP) design of the nRF9151 reduces PCB complexity and component
count.
• Nordic Semiconductor’s Ecosystem: Having Nordic Semiconductor’s nRF Connect
SDK, firmware support, and developer tools can help simplify firmware development.
• Good Market Availability and Long-Term Support: Ensures reliable supply chain and
ongoing vendor support for production. The SiP also comes at DKK 112.42 (Qty.
500), being a good price compared to competitors[86].
A overview of the nRF9151 SiP is shown in Figure 4.2.
Some of the closest competitors could be the Quectel BG95 and u-blox SARA-R5. These
modules are known for their NB-IoT and LTE-M connectivity and are commonly used in
IoT applications. The Quectel BG95 is a widely used cellular IoT module that supports
NB-IoT and LTE-M. Some versions of the IC also have an integrated GNSS receiver, just
like the nRF9151. Quectel also offers QuecPython, which is inherited from microPython,
36
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:59==
Figure 4.2: nRF9151 SiP overview showing the various internal components making up
the SiP[87].
Figure 4.3: Nordic Semiconductor’s comparison between battery life and positioning pre-
cision[88].
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
37
==PAGE:60==
as a programming environment in Python to run application code directly on the mod-
ule[89]. While, as discussed in Section 3.12, Python is a high-level interpreted language
that simplifies development, it typically results in lower runtime eﬀiciency compared to
compiled languages. In contrast, the nRF9151 is programmed using the nRF Connect
SDK, which is based on Zephyr RTOS, and should offer more control and eﬀiciency. An
alternative approach, is using an external MCU to run the application with a lower-level
language, while controlling the Quectal BG95 over UART. However, this increases PCB
complexity and component count.
The u-blox SARA-R5 series offers integrated GNSS and cellular support, also making it
a strong competitor. However, it lacks the SiP integration that reduces board space and
simplifies design, while also coming at a higher price than the nRF9151 at D̃KK 205.57
(Qty. 500)[90]. The SARA-R5 would also need to be controlled via AT commands over
UART, meaning an external MCU is required for running our application, and just as
for the Quectel BG95, this would increase PCB complexity and component count. The
schematics of our nRF9151 integration are shown in Appendix B.
4.2.2
nRF7000
To support Wi-Fi-based positioning, we selected the nRF7000 from Nordic Semiconduc-
tor. This low-power Wi-Fi 6 companion IC is specifically designed for active and passive
scanning of nearby access points, without supporting standard Wi-Fi communication. It
cannot connect to Wi-Fi networks or transmit data over the internet, but instead focuses
entirely on collecting SSID and signal strength information for positioning purposes. This
makes it highly suitable for battery-powered geolocation tracker devices like ours, where
Wi-Fi is used exclusively for location estimations.
The nRF7000 offers support for both 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz dual-band, low power con-
sumption, and is optimized for use with Nordic Semiconductor’s nRF91 series, making it
a good addition to our system built around the nRF9151 SiP. It is also fully supported
and integrated into Nordic Semiconductor’s software development platform, which helps
streamline development.
Therefore, the nRF7000 offers an eﬀicient method of adding
Wi-Fi-based positioning support to our tracker[91]. Based on examples from Nordic Semi-
conductor as seen in Figure 4.3, choosing which positioning technology to use can have a
large impact on battery life and precision, so having all the features of the nRF9151 com-
bined with an nRF70-series Wi-Fi Companion IC would provide a good range of options
to choose from.
The nRF7000 interfaces with the nRF9151 host via SPI. To further minimize power con-
sumption, we designed a dedicated power management path, where an LDO supplies clean,
stable voltage to the nRF7000 and a load switch between the LDO and the nRF7000 to
allow complete power gating when Wi-Fi scanning is not needed. This setup ensures the
IC is fully powered down outside of scanning events, reducing overall system power draw.
Integration schematics of the nRF7000 are shown in Appendix B.
4.2.3
nPM1300
After evaluating various power architectures and PMICs, we selected the nPM1300 from
Nordic Semiconductor to manage power in our device.
It integrates various features,
including dual buck converters, LDOs/load switches, battery charging, watchdog, hard
reset, and I2C configuration into a single compact chip. A block diagram of the nPM1300
is depicted in Figure 4.4. Using the nPM1300 helps reduce PCB complexity and overall
footprint compared to using multiple discrete components for obtaining the same features.
Compared to other PMICs and discrete implementations, the nPM1300 offers additional
advanced system management features such as battery fuel gauging, system-level watch-
38
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:61==
Figure 4.4: Block diagram of the nPM1300[92].
dog, hard reset, power loss warning, and failed-boot recovery[93]. This also adds an extra
layer of built-in safety and reliability.
One alternative we considered was a simpler discrete power architecture, e.g., using a
battery charging IC combined with a couple of LDO regulators and external MOSFETs
to switch individual power rails.
While this could reduce initial complexity, it would
eliminate features such as fuel gauging, system watchdog, power loss detection, and boot
recovery, or require multiple additional ICs to implement. This would also increase design
time, firmware development, while also adding to the complexity of manual testing and
validation, and a higher risk of integration bugs when the PCB has been produced. At
the early stages of development, it was also diﬀicult to determine which features were
”must-haves” versus ”nice-to-haves.” Therefore, choosing a flexible, all-in-one PMIC gave
us some headroom. The device is offered in a Wafer-Level packaging (3.1 x 2.4 mm) and
a QFN packaging (5 x 5 mm).
The nPM1300 provides good power eﬀiciency, with a quiescent current of 500 nA in hi-
bernate and 800 nA when running off battery power with no loads[92]. We verified the
performance using the Nordic Thingy:91 X development board, which features both the
nRF9151 and nPM1300. Here, the whole development boards current consumption was
measured at approximately 13 µA with all outputs disabled and the nRF9151 in sleep
mode. In hibernation, the current draw dropped to around 600 nA for the whole system.
This outperforms alternatives like the MAX77654 (Analog Devices), specifying a quies-
cent current of 28 µA in normal-power mode, 6–13 µA in low-power mode[94]. In addition
to performance, the cost advantage, priced at DKK 9.01 per 1000 units[95] (DKK 22.65
for MAX77654[96]) and full support in the Nordic Semiconductor’s software development
platform further contributed to our decision.
The nPM1300 also supports both hibernate and ship modes, supporting deep sleep and
full battery isolation from the system[97]. Hibernate mode keeps the internal timer active
and allows timed wakeups, supporting configurable sleep periods of up to 74.5 hours[98].
Nordic Semiconductor also claims that the nPM1300’s regulators are more eﬀicient than
the internal regulators or LDOs typically found in SiPs like the nRF9151, showing over
20% extended battery life when using nPM1300’s regulators compared to internal SiP
LDOs[93].
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
39
==PAGE:62==
All things considered, the nPM1300 provided the best trade-off between integration, eﬀi-
ciency, and cost when compared with simpler discrete architectures or competing PMICs.
Fuel gauging
As mentioned above, one of the features of the nPM1300 is fuel gauging. Fuel gauging
is the method of determining the state of charge of batteries. The nPM1300 includes an
algorithm-based fuel gauge, which estimates the battery’s state of charge from 0-100% by
using a virtual battery model and real-time measurements of voltage, temperature, and
current. This provides high accuracy with ultra-low power consumption (3̃ µA), and unlike
traditional coulomb counters, it does not draw current during system-off. The fuel gauge
algorithm is running on the host MCU, while the virtual battery model can be made using
the nPM1300 Evaluation Kit, the nPM Fuel Gauge board, and the nPM PowerUP app.
Compared to a simpler method like open circuit voltage measuring, which suffers from
low accuracy and temperature sensitivity, this solution offers the best balance of accuracy,
cost-eﬀiciency, and power savings[99].
4.2.4
Antenna Selection
Figure 4.5: Example of layout with a printed λ/4 monopole antenna, from [100].
Choosing the right antennas was an important part of our hardware design, as it has
a direct impact on the performance, size, and reliability of the wireless communication
system. Since our device needs to support multiple radio technologies, including GNSS,
LTE-M/NB-IoT, and Wi-Fi, we looked into several types of antennas to understand their
strengths, weaknesses, and how well they would fit our use case. This section goes through
the main antenna types we considered, comparing their eﬀiciency, size, and integration
complexity.
Printed Monopole
The simplest antenna to create would be a monopole, as it is simply a conductor, e.g., wire
or trace on a printed circuit board of the correct length. A conventional monopole antenna
has an input impedance of 37.5Ω. An example of a 2.4 GHz antenna to be printed on FR4
material has been described by Nordic Semiconductor in their white paper[100]. While
this design is very cheap to produce, it would take up too much space in our application,
as this would require a large area. The required size increases at lower frequencies, making
40
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:63==
(a)
Stamped
metal
an-
tenna
(Abracon
PRO-OB-
536)[102].
(b) Flex cable Wi-Fi antenna
(Molex 1461530100).
(c)
Ceramic
chip
antenna
(TDK ANT16244DT)[103].
Figure 4.6: Examples of Wi-Fi antennas: metal stamped, flex cable, and ceramic chip
types.
it even less practical for LTE-M or GNSS. An example from the white paper can be seen
in Figure 4.5.
Printed Inverted F Antenna
Compared to the monopole, a meandered Inverted F Antenna (IFA) takes up less space,
due to its meandered design. The IFA behaves much like a monopole; the design is a mix
of an intermediate position-fed quarter-wavelength monopole antenna and an inverted-L
antenna[101]. An example of an IFA is seen in Figure 4.9a. Another advantage of IFA
compared to a monopole is that the position feeding of the antenna allows it to have an
input impedance of 50Ω. A disadvantage is that this antenna design can require tuning
depending on the PCB layout, and it is complex to create a wide-/dual-band antenna as
the design gets increasingly complex.
Stamped Metal Antenna
A mass-manufactured, non-printed option of the IFA is a Stamped Metal Antenna. This
type of antenna consists of a stamped and folded piece of metal that can be soldered onto
the PCB. It has the same advantage as the IFA and is cheap, at 1.64 DKK per piece at
1000 pieces[102] for a 2.4/5.5 GHz model from Abracon[102]. However, for our application,
the stamped metal antenna is not suitable, as it occupies too much PCB area and adds
excessive volume due to its height. An example of such an antenna from Abracon is shown
in Figure 4.6a.
Flex Cabled Antenna
Flex cabled antennas are a convenient option to choose if space is not an issue. They are
very thin, but otherwise take up a large area, especially when needed to support multiple
radio technologies. At lower frequencies, like the lower bands of LTE (~700 MHz), antenna
size increases due to the inverse relationship between frequency and physical length. These
antennas are usually terminated with a cable from the manufacturer, so they are convenient
to use with a U.FL connector on the PCB, and therefore occupying minimal space on the
PCB itself. They come with an adhesive backing for easy mounting. They should not be
mounted too close to ground as it impacts their performance as seen in Figure 4.7 and
Figure 4.8[104]. A Wi-Fi flex cable antenna from Molex can be seen in Figure 4.6b.
Ceramic Chip Antenna
A smaller and more modern alternative is a ceramic chip antenna. Ceramic chip antennas
are compact surface-mount components that use ceramic materials to create an antenna.
These SMD components are typically low-cost, small in size, and require relatively simple
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
41
==PAGE:64==
Figure 4.7: Return loss of flex cable antenna at 2.4 GHz at four locations with parallel
plane ground, Figure 6.1.2 from [104].
Figure 4.8: Return loss of flex cable antenna at 5 GHz at four locations with parallel plane
ground, Figure 6.1.3 from [104].
42
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:65==
tuning and matching networks to achieve acceptable performance [103]. An example of a
ceramic chip antenna for 2.4/5 GHz applications is shown in Figure 4.6c.
However, when it comes to applications such as LTE-M and NB-IoT, ceramic chip anten-
nas present notable limitations. Supporting lower frequency bands (e.g., 700–900 MHz)
typically requires a physically larger ceramic antenna, which can negate the space-saving
benefits. Additionally, achieving acceptable performance at these lower frequencies often
depends on the availability of a suﬀiciently large ground plane, which again complicates
the integration for our desired form factor of the PCB. These constraints make the ceramic
chip antennas less suitable for small, multi-band LTE-M and NB-IoT devices, especially
if we want to target global coverage.
Patch Antenna
Patch antennas, also called microstrip antennas, are commonly used in GNSS and satellite
systems because they offer good performance in a compact and flat design. They work by
placing a metal patch on top of a dielectric layer, with a ground plane underneath. This
setup gives the antenna a directional radiation pattern, which is useful for e.g. receiving
signals from satellites above. They are typically designed for one specific frequency, mean-
ing they are not ideal if you need to cover multiple bands, like in LTE-M or NB-IoT, unless
you add extra components or use multiple antennas. Patch antennas are physically larger
than compact alternatives like chip or trace antennas, especially at lower frequencies, be-
cause they need to be proportionally big, about half the wavelength, to achieve resonance
at the operating frequency. For small mobile devices, a highly directional antenna may
degrade performance when the device is rotated or flipped during use. On the upside,
these types of patches can be easily printed right onto your PCB, and no extra tuning
and/or matching network is often required[105][106].
Virtual Antenna®
Ignion has created what they call an ”antenna-less” technology, also called Virtual Antenna®.
This method replaces traditional antenna elements with compact, multiband antenna
boosters. These boosters resemble chip antennas in form factor but are up to 10 times
smaller than conventional designs, according to Ignion[107]. Unlike traditional antennas,
which radiate primarily from the antenna structure itself with some contribution from
the ground plane, the Virtual Antenna® concept flips this dynamic. Ignion explains that
the antenna booster acts as a non-resonant component that redirects the signal to the
ground plane, which becomes the primary radiating structure. This also means the an-
tenna booster elements are very wideband and can support multiple standards, where the
only requirement is to adapt the matching network for the specific use case and frequency
bands[107].
According to Ignion, their antenna boosters provide a wider bandwidth1 and flatter re-
sponse than antennas that are based on geometry, such as the printed and stamped metal
antennas, while also also being more eﬀicient in the same area. Ignion also claims that
the increased total radiated power from their antenna boosters can help save power on
battery-powered devices.
In essence, according to Ignion, their Virtual Antennas® are
optimal for use in small, battery-powered devices such as IoT trackers and sensors[107].
Comparing the Antenna Types
To identify the most suitable antennas for our design, we compared the size, cost, expected
peak gain, and bandwidth of each antenna type.

trace, stamped metal, ceramic, and Ignion’s Virtual Antenna® options. We also created
visual mockups as seen in Figure 4.9.
Flex-cable antennas were ruled out early in the process, primarily due to the space they
require, not only in terms of footprint but especially in height, as also described earlier
in this section. For example, as shown in Figure 4.7, a Wi-Fi antenna operating at 2.4
GHz would need at least 15 mm of separation from the parallel ground plane to achieve
acceptable performance3, which would increase the height of the device too much for our
wanted requirement.
We also evaluated an IFA PCB trace antenna, based on design recommendations from
a Texas Instruments white paper[108]. As discussed in Section 4.2.4, the IFA offers a
compact and cost-effective solution for 2.4 GHz Wi-Fi.
However, the design becomes
increasingly complex when supporting multiple bands such as 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz, and
requires tuning depending on the final PCB layout. For that reason, we retained the IFA
in our comparison but ultimately considered other options more practical.
When inspecting the needed area for the different antenna on the mockups shown in
Figure 4.9, the stamped metal antennas quickly proved too large for our needs.
The
stamped metal LTE-M/NB-IoT antenna alone takes up 1260mm2, being more than half of
the wanted PCB size. In contrast, the ceramic antennas provided the smallest footprints
and relatively low cost, but in our comparison, Ignion’s Virtual Antenna® technology
consistently offered better or comparable gain for the three technologies.
Ignion also
offered design guides for integrating multiple antennas on a small PCB. The design guides
explained how the LTE and GNSS antennas could be placed on the same clearance area
to save space.
The guides also provided details on how to optimize LTE-M/NB-IoT
performance using an RF switch and multiple matching networks, including practical
layouts for Nordic chipsets like the nRF9160[119]. All of Ignion’s antenna booster elements
are also ultra-wideband, meaning they can be modified and tuned to specific needs for
future iterations, as they are not designed for specific frequency bands.
Given these considerations, as well as interest in the technology, we selected Ignion’s
Virtual Antenna® models for Wi-Fi (NN02-101), GNSS (NN03-320), and LTE-M/NB-IoT
(NN03-310) in our final design, depicted in Figure 4.12.
4.2.5
Sensors
Our system includes two onboard sensors: the Bosch BME688 environmental sensor and
the Analog Devices ADXL367 ultra-low power accelerometer. These components were
selected based on their combination of functionality, power eﬀiciency, footprint size, and
support for Nordic Semiconductor’s software development platform.
BME688 - Environmental Sensor
The BME688 is a highly integrated environmental sensor capable of measuring tempera-
ture, humidity, pressure, and gas. This single-chip solution offers good value by providing
multiple sensor functions in one package, reducing board space, while maintaining many
sensor functionalities[120]. The BME688 sensor is depicted in Figure 4.10a.
The BME688 also supports a low-power forced mode for single-shot measurements, which
makes it suitable for infrequent sampling in power-constrained systems like ours. Fur-
thermore, it communicates over I2C or SPI, integrating easily with our design without
requiring additional bus complexity[120].

Environmental data might not be the core functionality of our tracking device, but it adds
valuable information that could be used for future smart applications such as monitoring
storage conditions, detecting unusual environmental changes, or assisting in power-saving
features based on this data, and having all the sensor functionalities of the BME688 will
not limit future development of these features.
ADXL367 – Ultra-Low Power Accelerometer
The ADXL367 is an ultra-low power, 3-axis microelectromechanical systems (MEMS)
accelerometer, with 0.89 µA consumption at a 100 Hz output data rate, 180 nA motion
wake-up mode, and only 40 nA standby current, making it excellent for power-constrained
devices. In addition to low power consumption, the sensor comes in a small package (2.2
× 2.3 × 0.87 mm), allowing us to integrate it into our compact PCB layout without
increasing the footprint of the board, and also supports both I2C and SPI, which means
that it can also be easily integrated with our design without requiring additional bus
complexity[122]. The ADXL367 sensor is depicted in Figure 4.10b.
Just like the environmental sensor, this is not the main feature and focus of our geolocation
tracking device, but it gives the capabilities of developing features for our system to detect
relevant motion events autonomously, waking up the main system only when necessary,
which could help extend battery life, or be appropriate for certain use cases.
4.3
PCB Design and Layout
The PCB for the ultra-low power tracker was designed using Altium Designer. The overall
goal was to achieve a compact, manufacturable, and reliable design with good RF per-
formance, while meeting the requirements we set for the project at the beginning of this
thesis.
To accelerate development and ensure good design practices, we used publicly available
hardware design files from Nordic Semiconductor as references. These included schematics
and layout files for the nPM1300, nRF7000, and several Nordic development kits such as
the nRF9151DK, Thingy:91, and Thingy:91 X. These references helped guide component
placement, RF layout, and integration of various subsystems in our own custom PCB
design. Our final PCB design is depicted in Figure 4.11, with larger 3D models, and each
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
47

copper layer is depicted in Appendix A. The schematics and Bill Of Materials (BOM) of
the whole design can be seen in Appendix B and Appendix E respectively.
4.3.1
Substrate and Stack-up
The PCB was designed using a standard FR-4 substrate. This was to balance cost, manu-
facturability, and performance. FR-4 substrate is not ideal for high-frequency performance
when compared to specialized low-loss materials like Rogers laminate, which are typically
also significantly more expensive than the FR-4 substrate.
Although Roger laminates
would provide better RF performance, most of our RF signals are at the beginning of
a few gigahertz, where the highest frequencies would be the 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz (range
spans from 5.15 GHz to 5.85 GHz) used for Wi-Fi. It is generally accepted that FR-4
can provide acceptable RF performance for frequencies below 6 GHz, particularly when
transmission lines are kept short[123]. As our entire device is about the size of a credit
card, with the PCB itself being even smaller, the RF trace lengths will be kept relatively
short. This should help reduce the negative effects of the higher losses of FR-4 at higher
frequencies.
It is also worth mentioning that the Wi-Fi frequencies (the highest frequencies, being the
most critical) will only be used for Wi-Fi scanning. The nRF7000 IC will not require high-
precision RF transmission or reception, unlike a dedicated Wi-Fi communication system.
The scanning function only involves detecting access points and their signal strength,
rather than transmitting or receiving large amounts of data at high throughput.
To keep the PCB as small as possible and to keep it within our requirements, we decided
to mount components on each side of the PCB. To separate high-frequency signal routing,
ground, and power, we started out designing with a six-layer stack-up.
The six-layer
structure offered reasonable signal integrity and suﬀicient layer allocation for our early
needs. The layers were organized as follows:
• Top Layer: RF signal routing and components.
48
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:71==
• Inner Layer 1: Continuous ground plane.
• Inner Layer 2: Power, low-speed signals, and less critical traces
• Inner Layer 3: Low-speed signal routing and less critical traces.
• Inner Layer 4: Continuous ground plane.
• Bottom Layer: Signal routing and components.

However, as the design progressed, we started looking into PCB fabrication options and
explored the different six-layer stack-ups offered by our end-manufacturer, JLCPCB. We
realized that the standard six-layer stack-up had a pretty short spacing between Inner
Layers 2 and 3, which is not ideal for signal integrity. While JLCPCB does offer better
six-layer stack-ups with improved layer spacing, those come with added cost.
Fortunately, JLCPCB also offered an eight-layer stack-up (JLC08161H-2116) at the same
price point as the six-layer. This allowed us to transition to an eight-layer stack-up, which
provided better layer spacing without affecting the cost. We decided to keep our original
layer assignments for routing and power as they were. This was to avoid having to redo
our initial layout and reroute the entire board. However, this was still a time-consuming
process.
For the two extra layers, we purely used them as additional ground planes.
The eight-layer stack-up offers thinner dielectric spacing between each layer compared
to the six-layer, meaning our signal and power layers are now closer to a ground plane.
This, together with the extra ground layers, should help reduce radiation, crosstalk and
improve overall signal integrity. The stack-up is described in Table 4.4, where the final
layer configuration ended up as follows:
• Top Layer (L1): RF signal routing and components.
• Inner Layer 1 (L2): Continuous ground plane.
• Inner Layer 2 (L3): Power, low-speed signals, and less critical traces
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
49
==PAGE:72==
• Inner Layer 3 (L4): Continuous ground plane.
• Inner Layer 4 (L5): Continuous ground plane.
• Inner Layer 5 (L6): Low-speed signal routing and less critical traces.
• Inner Layer 6 (L7): Continuous ground plane.
• Bottom Layer (L8): Signal routing and components.
4.3.2
Antenna PCB implementation
(a) Wi-Fi antenna (Ignion NN02-101)[111].
(b) GNSS antenna (Ignion NN03-320)[117].
(c) LTE-M/NB-IoT antenna (Ignion NN03-
310)[114].
Figure 4.12: Selected antennas used in the system: (a) Wi-Fi, (b) GNSS, and (c) LTE-
M/NB-IoT.
The PCB integrates the three chip antennas mentioned in Section 4.2.4 from Ignion uti-
lizing their Virtual Antenna® Technology. We will be using the TRIO mXTEND (NN03-
310) for LTE-M/NB-IoT, DUO mXTEND (NN03-320) for GNSS, and NANO mXTEND
(NN02-101) for Wi-Fi scanning, all depicted in Figure 4.12.
Initially, we used Ignion’s Oxion platform to explore antenna placement. The tool allows
you to define a PCB size, select antenna booster models, and target bands. The tool
will then use AI/Machine Learning and generate an initial design report with matching
networks and layout. However, the suggested layout was not physically compatible with
our intended PCB size, as we would have insuﬀicient space for the rest of our components.
The suggested design is seen in Figure 4.13. The Oxion platform also supports review of
your final design.
To reduce space used by the antennas, we adopted the layout recommended in Ignion’s
design guide Design Guide to cellular IoT antenna integration (with Nordic nRF9160).
This guide proposes placing the GNSS and LTE-M/NB-IoT antenna boosters within the
same cutout area when using a PCB of nearly similar size as our intended size[119].
50
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:73==
Figure 4.13: Suggested PCB layout using Oxion from Ignion[124].
Throughout the design, we followed the general layout guidelines recommended on the
Oxion platform and Design Guide, including:
• Maintaining the recommended clearance area around each antenna free from ground
planes, copper, and components across all layers.
• Placing antennas near the PCB corners.
• Arrange pads for matching network to host 0402/0603 components as close as pos-
sible to the antenna feed point, with high-Q components and tight tolerances.
• Have a least one continuous ground plane and proper via stitching across layers.
• Ensuring 50 Ωimpedance transmission lines between the matching network and RF
chips.
For RF routing, we implemented two types of grounded coplanar waveguide (GCPW)
transmission lines:
• GCPW with reference between Top Layer (L1) and Inner Layer 1 (L2)
• GCPW with reference between Top Layer (L1) and Inner Layer 2 (L3)
Having two types of GCPW allowed us to make better transitions to pins of different
components or the matching networks. The trace widths and gap distances were calculated
using the Impedance Calculator from JLCPCB with the stack-up described in Section
4.3.1, ensuring 50 Ωimpedance for both configurations. We also placed extra pads with
either 0 Ωresistor or as not connected for all matching circuits, to ensure we could tune
the systems once manufactured.
Our final design was uploaded for review by engineers on Oxion on May 8th, 2025, but at
the time of writing, the case is still open and under review, as seen in Figure 4.14.
TRIO mXTEND (NN03-310) – LTE-M/NB-IoT
The TRIO mXTEND (NN03-310) antenna booster element, depicted in Figure 4.12c, will
be used exclusively for LTE-M and NB-IoT communication. The system should be tuned
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
51
==PAGE:74==
Figure 4.14: Oxion project overview [124].
to cover both the 689-960 MHz band and 1710-2200 MHz band, ensuring global roaming
support for LTE-M and NB-IoT[125][126][127][128].
For the antenna integration, we have followed the Design Guide to cellular IoT antenna
integration (with Nordic nRF9160) provided by Ignion. As previously mentioned in Section
4.2.4, the performance of the Virtual Antenna® Technology is influenced by the size and
shape of the ground plane. To achieve optimal performance across the needed frequency
bands to ensure global coverage, the Design Guide recommends an active matching solution
rather than a passive one for small PCB sizes, like in our case[119].
For the active matching, Ignion recommends using an RF switch to toggle between multiple
matching networks, with the Qorvo QM13345 being the suggested component, as illus-
trated in Figure 4.16. Our connected RF Module (nRF9151) will dynamically manage
band switching via the MIPI RFFE interface, which also controls the RF switch to select
between different matching networks tailored for specific frequency bands. A performance
comparison of the passive and active matching approaches is shown in Figure 4.15[119].
As seen in the figure, the active solution provides significantly better performance in the
lower frequency bands compared to the passive solution. Using the active solution will not
only enable dynamic selection of optimized matching networks for different LTE bands
but also give us the flexibility to fine-tune each matching network individually. This ap-
proach allows for targeted optimization of specific bands or future expansion to support
additional LTE bands as needed.
To optimize performance, we followed Ignion’s layout recommendations by placing the
antenna booster element at the edge of the PCB with a ground clearance zone of approx-
imately 45 x 16 mm. To minimize surface currents and coupling, we included a row of
ground vias around the perimeter of the clearance zone. Our design of the switch and
matching network closely follows Ignion’s recommendations, based on both the Ignion De-
sign Guide (see Figure 4.17) and the publicly available hardware reference designs from
52
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:75==
Figure 4.15: Comparison between passive and active antenna matching solutions for small
PCB designs, showing total eﬀiciency (%). The active solution offers improved perfor-
mance across multiple frequency bands by dynamically adapting the matching network
via the RF module[119].
Figure 4.16: Recommended RF switch configuration for antenna matching to control
multiple matching networks[119].
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
53
==PAGE:76==
Figure 4.17: Recommended antenna component (NN03-310) and layout guidelines for
optimal performance across LTE/NB-IoT frequency bands, as advised by Ignion’s design
guide[119].
Nordic Semiconductor, using them as a practical reference to align our layout and RF
traces with a proven and validated configuration. Our finished design is shown in Fig-
ure 4.18. The trace connecting from the LTE-M/NB-IoT modem (nRF9151) to the RF
front end is routed as a GCPW, with 50 Ωimpedance. We have placed a U.FL connector
on the RF trace to be able to measure the matching or use of an external antenna if
needed.
Despite following best practices for antenna placement and impedance matching, we en-
countered several issues with the Qorvo QM13345 during our development process. First,
Qorvo does not provide a publicly available datasheet for the QM133454, making it dif-
ficult to evaluate its electrical characteristics and integration requirements. Additionally,
at the time of sourcing, the component was not available from major distributors. Since
the QM13345 is used on the Nordic Thingy:91 X development board, we attempted to
probe and characterize the component in-circuit, hoping to understand its behavior. Un-
fortunately, this method offered limited insight due to inaccessible internal connections
and the lack of documentation.
We tried to find possible replacements, but alternative RF switches either lacked the same
functionality, had incompatible control interfaces, or were similarly unavailable. Although
some alternative RF switches could be a suitable fit, we did not want to risk integrating a
substitute without knowing if it truly matched the electrical behavior of the QM13345. To
avoid potential mismatch or degraded performance, we ultimately decided to de-solder the
Qorvo switch from our Thingy:91 X board and re-solder it onto our PCB for prototyping.
To support the cellular connectivity, we included a traditional nano-SIM card socket in
our design. We chose this solution to allow easy swapping of SIM cards and to avoid
e-waste associated with soldered SIMs.
4We also tried contacting Qorvo multiple times without any response.

DUO mXTEND (NN03-320) - GNSS
The DUO mXTEND (NN03-320) antenna booster, seen in Figure 4.12b, will be used for
GNSS reception on the frequency range around 1575 MHz, which should give us coverage
for both GPS and QZSS L1 band, as these are the constellations that the nRF9151 sup-
ports[129]. This booster was selected for its fairly small form factor of 7.0 x 3.0 x 2.0 mm,
but also without the need for any clearance beyond the antenna footprint, which allowed
us to place the antenna booster close to the LTE antenna booster element, without the
need for extra clearance space[130].
To optimize performance and following the general recommendation for Ignion, we place
the antenna booster element at the corner of the PCB, with a 1 mm width feeding line
into the matching network.
For the initial matching network, we followed the Design
Guide with the schematic layout seen in Figure 4.19. Ignion offers design files for some
of their antennas for different board sizes, and using Keysight ADS, we simulated various
matching network configurations5. We ended with the following values for the matching
network: Z1 = 2.8 nH, Z2 = 2.5 nH, and Z3 = 0Ω. This gave us the frequency response
shown in Figure 4.20, where proper impedance matching is indicated with under −6 dB
as a rule of thumb[131].
The matching network is connected to a SKY65943-11, GNSS Low-Noise Amplifier (LNA)
for amplification, with a 50 ΩGCPW. The SKY65943-11 is a front-end module with an
integrated LNA, pre-filter, and post-filter for GNSS receivers. An LNA amplifies the weak
GNSS signals received by the antenna before they are processed by the receiver (nRF9151),
while preserving the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) by adding minimal noise. This makes
the GNSS receiver more sensitive to satellite signals, which is needed for acquiring and
maintaining a reliable position fix, especially in challenging environments with weak signal
levels or partial obstructions[132][133].
5Design files for Keysight ADS were only available and working for the GNSS antenna, which is why
it is the only setup being simulated. We therefore placed U.FL connectors for LTE-M/NB-IoT and Wi-Fi
for measurement or use of external antennas if needed.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications

Figure 4.21: GNSS antenna and RF design, with matching network and LNA.
An LNA, when placed close to the antenna, also helps with the resistive routing loss
between the LNA and the GNSS receiver. Here, an LNA ensures that most of the signal
loss occurs after amplification, so the impact of the resistive losses is less critical. As
seen in Figure 4.21, we tried to fit the LNA as close to the matching network as possible;
however, due to other components and clearance from the Wi-Fi antenna, we were unable
to fit it all the way up to the matching network.
The SKY65943-11 also comes with integrated filters, which help the GNSS receiver become
less sensitive to interference from other sources on our PCB, like rejection for the cellular
and WLAN frequency bands, minimizing the number of external components required for
keeping this isolation. It also has integrated output matching and only needs one exter-
nal input matching inductor, which again minimizes the number of external components
needed. The LNA comes with a signal gain of 15 dB and a noise figure of 1.7 dB, while
having a low current consumption of 2.9 mA when active, 100 nA during shutdown, and
comes in a small package size of 2.5 x 2.5 mm. The SKY65943-11 is also is optimized to
operate at 1559 to 1606 MHz, making it ideal for our design[132]. The SKY65943-11 is al-
ready used on the nRF9151DK development board, meaning we also had a good reference
design for the LNA and the nRF9151[134].
The LNA is connected to the GNSS RF input on the nRF9151, again with a 50 ΩGCPW.
The whole GNSS front end can be seen in Figure 4.21.
NANO mXTEND (NN02-101) – Wi-Fi Scanning
For Wi-Fi scanning we are using the NANO mXTEND (NN02-101), depicted in Fig-
ure 4.12a, as the antenna booster element. This antenna supports wireless connectivity
protocols operating from 2.4 GHz to 10.6 GHz, making it ideal for identifying access points
across modern Wi-Fi infrastructure with 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz frequency bands[111].
During the design phase, we explored possible configurations and matching networks for
dual-band (2.4 + 5 GHz) operation using resources from Ignion and the Oxion platform.
However, no public references or application notes provided a verified design supporting
both bands simultaneously for the NANO mXTEND.

4.4
Wireless Charging Integration
This section explores the design process for integrating wireless charging into our design.
It begins by outlining our choice of Qi receiver IC and the necessary components for it, as
well as how their values are calculated. Subsequently, we will explore the design of custom
PCB coils.
For the battery, we opted for a Lithium-polymer (Li-Po) battery, due to the flat form
factor, lightweight, relatively high energy density, and availability in a wide range of custom
sizes[135]. The battery had a capacity of 680 mAh. Li-Po batteries typically operate with
a nominal voltage around 3.6–3.7 V, with a full charge voltage of approximately 4.2 V and
a cut-off voltage near 2.7-3.0 V. They also have a relatively low self-discharge of about
5% per month[136]. Our battery has built-in protection circuits to ensure safe operation.
These protection circuits include:
• Overvoltage protection to prevent charging when the battery transitions from constant-
current to constant-voltage state, and causing battery damage.
• Undervoltage cut-off to disconnect the load when voltage goes below the working
voltage to prevent battery damage.
• Overcurrent protection to detect short circuits or excessive load.
• Temperature sensing to prevent operation outside safe thermal limits.
4.4.1
Qi Receiver
To enable WPT, we selected the BQ51013B from Texas Instruments as our Qi-compatible
wireless power receiver IC. The BQ51013B has a small footprint, measuring only 4.5 x 3.5
mm, as shown in Figure 4.24. The IC does not have an integrated battery charger, which
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
59
==PAGE:82==
Figure 4.25: Simplified schematic of the BQ51013B as wireless power receiver and power
supply[138].
allows for the nPM1300 PMIC to handle the battery charging, and the BQ51013B will
just be configured to deliver a 5 V output. The receiver IC can provide a maximum output
of 5 W, which should be more than suﬀicient for our application[138]. To prevent heating
up our battery and components too much, we have limited the charging to 500 mA, as
our choice of battery capacity is also only 680 mAh. This means that the nPM1300 needs
to have the VBUSINLIM0 and VBUSINLIMSTARTUP registers set to around 500 mA to
avoid drawing too much current from the BQ51013B. When using Nordic Semiconductor’s
software development platform, these registers are configured by using the device tree as
described in Section 4.7.2.
A simple schematic of the BQ51013B when configured as a wireless power receiver and
power supply is depicted in Figure 4.25. Most of the components needed for this configu-
ration are provided in the datasheet of the BQ51013B, while the two capacitors, C1 and
C2, need to be calculated using physical measurements of the receiver coil, as they make
up the resonant circuit of the coil. Here, we need to measure the inductance of the coil
at 1 V RMS at a frequency of 100 kHz, one measurement with nothing near the inductor,
providing the free-space inductance, and one with the inductor in the actual configuration
it is to be used in. From the instructions in the BQ51013B datasheet, the equation for C1
and C2 is given by
C1 = [(fS2π)2L′
S]−1
[138]

and
C2 = [(fD2π)2LS −1

the operating environment (with battery, ferrite material, and 3D print), and LS is the
measured inductance of the receiver coil in free space. The BQ51013B also required the
coil to have a quality factor (Q-factor) greater than 77 at 1 MHz. The quality factor is
determined by
Q = 2πfDLS

presented in Section 3.10 and simulation tools. We therefore simply selected the most
optimal layout, even though it came with a lower Q-factor. We accepted this, hoping that
it would affect only the charging eﬀiciency, rather than the functional operation of the

As mentioned in Section 3.10, we wanted to reduce cost by designing our own PCB coils,

We chose a target
inductance of around 12 µH, as most of the recommended receiver coils from the BQ51013B
datasheet were around this inductance. To fit within our size requirements, we opted for
40 mm diameter spiral coils. To keep the cost at an absolute minimum, we opted for a
1-oz copper thickness and FR-4 substrate for the design. We designed several custom PCB
coils in Altium Designer based on our calculated values.

reducing the DC resistance.

ground (10 cm) should allow the simulation to resemble a measurement of free space on
the coil. The simulation setup can be seen in Figure 4.29b. The coils were simulated from
0 to 1.1 MHz, the results can be seen in Table 4.5.

4.4.3
Magnetic Charging Connector
As an alternative to wireless charging, we chose a magnetic charging connector with pogo
pins. These deliver a 5 V output, which can be used to charge our device. This would
also allow for the prototype device to take up a smaller area, at the cost of extra height,
by placing the battery below the main PCB. The magnetic charging connector is depicted
in Figure 4.30. As seen in Section 4.6, the prototype design with the magnetic charging
connector is taller but otherwise takes up less area compared to the wireless charging
version.
4.5
Prototyping and Manufacturing
This section will outline the practical decisions made during the transition from a PCB
design to a physical prototype, describing challenges found during this process.
4.5.1
Vias and Stackup
One of the first major challenges in prototyping was that our initial PCB layout was
made use of blind and buried vias to simplify routing in the compact multilayer design.
However, when requesting a manufacturing quote from PCBWay (as they supported these
via types), we discovered that just five bare PCBs would cost €372.74 (approx. DKK
2780) excluding shipping6, which far exceeded our budget.
To reduce expenses, we decided to redesign the board using only through-hole vias. While
this added routing complexity, it made the design compatible with standard processes at
JLCPCB, bringing the price for five PCBs down to €60.64 (approx. DKK 452)7, which
aligned way better within our budget constraints. At the same time, although we initially
planned to use a six-layer stackup (as noted in Section 4.3.1), we opted for an eight-
layer stackup instead. This change allowed us to avoid additional material fees, and the
estimated price for five boards was slightly lower at €57.84 (approx. DKK 431)8.

7Price updated as of May 1st, 2025
8Price updated as of May 15th, 2025

4.5.2
Assembly
Initially, we considered using JLCPCB for the full assembly of both sides of our PCB
prototype. However, this quickly proved impractical. Several components had minimum
order quantities for assembly, meaning we would have had to order five fully assembled
boards. Additionally, many components were not available as standard components at
JLCPCB or were out of stock at their Global Parts Ordering service and required pre-
purchasing, with estimated lead times of approximately 14 days. With the design requiring
two-sided assembly, additional setup and feeder loading fees brought the total cost to
approximately €600 (DKK 4475) for five prototypes, again well beyond our budget for
this project.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
65
==PAGE:88==
To reduce the high cost of two-sided PCB assembly, we revisited the layout and moved
as many components as possible to a single side. This allowed JLCPCB to handle the
majority of the assembly on the bottom side of the PCB, using parts they had in stock.
The remaining components, both for the top and bottom sides, were ordered from Mouser
and DigiKey, and were kindly hand-soldered by Pranom Andreasen from Cobham Satcom.
These included some of the more complex components, such as the nRF9151, nPM1300,
and nRF7000. The setup used for soldering can be seen in Appendix C. Afterwards, we
manually soldered connectors for UART to enable debugging. The final prototype board
is depicted in Figure 4.31. We also soldered the Qi receiver circuit by hand; the result is
seen in Figure 4.32.
The cost for JLCPCB to manufacture 5 raw PCBs and assemble the majority of the
bottom side of 3 PCBs came in at a price of €281.25, including shipping, and in addition
to this, DKK 754 were added to the cost in the form of customs and customs fees. This
also included the cost of 5 raw PCBs for the Qi-receiver (approx. €2.63). Components for
three Qi-receiver PCBs as well as three of our main prototype PCBs were ordered from
DigiKey and Mouser at a total cost of DKK 1296. This brought the total cost of the 3
final prototype devices to DKK 4286.69.
To evaluate the viability of future scaling, we also requested a quote from JLCPCB
for 1,000 fully assembled boards; we found the resulting cost would be approximately
€57,1549, giving a unit price of approximately DKK 426. These prices include the price of
the non-stocked components in the JLCPCBs Global Parts Ordering service. We consider
this a fair price for the complexity of our design.
4.6
3D Printed Cases
When we had the final PCB design completed in Altium Designer, we exported the PCBs
as .STEP files and imported them into Onshape. This allowed us to design a prototype
3D model for each of the two configurations, a version with wireless charging and a version
with a magnetic charging connector. When the prototype 3D models were completed, the
files were exported as .STL files and 3D prints using resin were ordered from PCBWay.
The two final prototype designs can be seen in Figure 4.34, and the placement of the PCBs
inside can be seen in Figure 4.33. Holes have been cut out for the LEDs, BME688 sensor,
and SIM card holder, as well as to access buttons and the power switch using a paperclip.
The cases are designed as a minimum viable prototype where there has not been optimized
for form, fit, and function. The printed enclosures with the final PCB prototypes, and in
respect to a credit card, can be seen in Figure 4.35. Dimensions for the Qi charge version
is 88.28 x 54.48 x 10.42 mm, while it is 55.57 x 50.35 x 15.05 mm for the magnetic charge
version.
4.7
Developing with nRF Connect SDK
As our device is based on the nRF9151 from Nordic Semiconductor, we have used the nRF
Connect SDK to develop our software. The nRF Connect SDK is Nordic Semiconductor’s
Software Development Kit (SDK) that is designed for creating application firmware that
utilizes Nordic Semiconductor’s series of SoCs and SiPs, i.e., nRF52, nRF53, nRF70, and
nRF91 series. The SDK provides a customizable framework to create software for the
nRF series of devices and has a wide range of samples from a simple Hello World to
more advanced Device Tracking examples. The SDK incorporates the Zephyr Real-Time
Operating System (RTOS) along with protocol stacks, libraries, and hardware drivers[85].

While not necessary for our project, the nRF Connect SDK also provides integration with
the HomeKit Accessory Development Kit; this allows developers to create products that
utilize HomeKit over Thread and HomeKit over Bluetooth Low Energy, thereby creating
battery-operated products compatible with the Apple Home ecosystem[85].
For our project, the nRF Connect SDK, along with the Nordic Developer Academy[139]
online learning platform and support forum on Nordic DevZone[140], allowed us to jump
in headfirst and have development kits running from day one. Through the online courses,
we were able to quickly learn how to interact with various peripherals, including I2C,
SPI, LTE-M/NB-IoT modem, GNSS positioning, as well as coexistence with the nRF7000
Wi-Fi Companion IC and nRF1300 PMIC.
As an example, for the GNSS, minimal configuration is required, and the nrf_modem_gnss
module abstracts the process of initiating a fix, handles event callbacks, and retrieves
either NMEA-formatted strings or structured PVT (Position, Velocity, Time) data. This
has allowed us to incorporate GNSS positioning with quite limited coding needed.
Additionally, we learned how to build, flash, and debug firmware while using the nRF
Connect for VS Code extension. This gave us a strong foundation for working eﬀiciently
with the SDK.
4.7.1
Building and Developing with Custom Board
Developing firmware for a custom hardware platform requires integration with the build
system and tools of the nRF Connect SDK. The SDK provides a good abstraction layer
that makes it possible to target multiple boards with the same application code, provided
the hardware is described correctly through board files. nRF Connect SDK is designed for
flexibility and portability across different hardware platforms. Unlike traditional embed-
ded workflows, where hardware configurations are embedded in C headers, the SDK uses
a combination of devicetree, Kconfig, and metadata files to define each hardware target.
To work effectively with our custom PCB, we had to create a custom board definition that
describes the hardware layout and supported peripherals[141].
While the nRF Connect SDK provides built-in support for many Nordic Semiconductor
development kits, these board definitions are tailored to the specific hardware on each
development kit. This meant that, since our PCB uses the nRF9151 SiP and custom
configurations of pins, flash, sensors, and other subsystems, none of the default board files
would work without modification. Therefore, we needed to create dedicated custom board
files to match our schematics. This should ensure proper initialization, driver configura-
tion, and allow us to easily switch between hardware revisions or test variants using the
flexible build system.
4.7.2
Boards Definitions and Hardware Model
As of version 2.9.0 of the nRF Connect SDK, the default system used for board definitions
is called HardWare Model v2(HWMv2) [142]. In HWMv2, a board is described by a set
of mandatory files located in a dedicated folder, such as:
boards/<vendor>/<board_name>/
￿￿￿board.yml
￿￿￿Kconfig.<board_name>
￿￿￿<board_name>.dts
￿￿￿<board_name>-pinctrl.dtsi
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
69
==PAGE:92==
Figure 4.36: Custom board selected in drop-down menu for building firmware using the
nRF Connect VS Code extension.
board.yml defines the properties of the board with name, SoC, revisions, and variants.
Kconfig.<board_name> serves the purpose of selecting the SoC(s) used by the board and
therefore pulls the appropriate hardware layer files for the SoC.<board_name>.dts is the
board-level devicetree file that describes the physical hardware layout of the board, in-
cluding components such as LEDs, buttons, connectors, and communication peripherals.
It also defines memory partitioning and can be structured into multiple files for easier
readability and maintainability. <board_name>-pinctrl.dtsi defines the pin-mapping of
all the board’s peripherals. Besides the mandatory files, there is also multiple optional
files. Most importantly for our project has been <board_name>_defconfig and Kcon-
fig.defconfig. The first one is a Kconfig fragment that is merged into the final build of
any application build for the board and therefore holds the bare minimum needed to be
enabled for the system to function, while the other file contain definitions of board-specific
default values for Kconfig options[141].
The nRF Connect for VS Code offers a GUI to create board files from scratch. However,
For our custom board, we primarily followed the method of duplicating and modifying
existing board files rather than creating one from scratch. Since our hardware was based
on the same SoC as the nRF9151DK and shared similarities with the Nordic Thingy:91 X,
we used those board definitions as a baseline. We copied the relevant board files into a new
folder, renamed them to reflect our custom board, and then modified the devicetree, pin
control, and configuration files to match our schematic and intended functionality[141].
The most diﬀicult thing to integrate in our board files was the external SPI flash chip (the
IS25WP128). The flash needed to be integrated in the devicetree, and is connected via the
SPI3 peripheral of the nRF9151. We therefore created a node under the SPI peripheral
to describe the connection and flash-specific parameters. The external flash is defined as
a child node named flash_ext, and it includes several important properties:
• compatible = "jedec,spi-nor": Identifies the flash as a JEDEC-compliant SPI
NOR device.
• size = <16777216>: Defines the flash size as 16 MB.
• jedec-id = [9d 70 18]: Specifies the JEDEC ID for the IS25WP128.
• sfdp-bfp = [...]: Includes the Basic Flash Parameter table from SFDP, required
by the driver.
• has-dpd: Indicates support for deep power-down mode.
70
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:93==
Figure 4.37: Clip-on SWD adapter for easy programming without needing to solder on
pins.
• t-enter-dpd = <4000>: Specifies 4 µs (in nanoseconds) for entering deep power-
down.
• t-exit-dpd = <20000>: Specifies 20 µs (in nanoseconds) for exiting deep power-
down.
These values are all found in the datasheet of the flash[143]. The sfdp-bfp stands for
Serial Flash Discoverable Parameters-Basic Flash Parameter, given in Table 5.3 in the
datasheet of the flash. This parameter describes flash behavior such as erase instructions,
supported commands, and memory layout directly into the devicetree, and therefore no
need for runtime retrieval of these parameters[144].
With the board files in place, building and flashing applications should be fairly straight-
forward. However, to build an application targeting our custom board we needed to specify
the location of the board files. This is done via the nRF Connect Extension for VS Code
by navigating to File -> Preferences -> Settings -> Extensions -> nRF Connect
-> Board Roots and adding the directory of your board files. When choosing a build
configuration for your application, you should be able to choose your custom board in
the board target drop-down as seen in Figure 4.36 with our custom board target named
mint_v2/nrf9151/ns.
4.7.3
Power On and First Boot
Before connecting power to the prototype board, the supply pins on the battery and
power connectors were checked for shorts. To prepare for the first power-up of the device,
a splitter cable for the battery was made.
This cable, shown in Figure 4.38, made it
possible to measure the current drawn from the battery using the Nordic Semiconductor
Power Profiler Kit II. For flashing the firmware and debugging during development, a
clip-on SWD adapter was used with a custom-made adapter cable for J-Link, as shown in
Figure 4.37. The initial power-up showed no signs of faults, with the average current draw
measured at around 8 mA. For comparison, the nRF9151 current draw on the nRF9151DK
had previously been measured at 2–4 mA without any power-saving techniques. Given
that the 8 mA current draw included the entire board without any configuration, this
value was considered normal and not indicative of any issues.
With our board files configured as described in Section 4.7.2, we flashed a sample Hello
World program. There were some complications with the custom board files in the SDK;
first, the path for the nsib_signing_key.pem file for secure signing of the software was a
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
71
==PAGE:94==
Figure 4.38: Custom battery adapter cable, with male and female 3-pin 1 mm pitch JST
connections. The power connections can be split at the DuPont connectors.
generic path in the SDK. To keep things simple, we started by using Nordic Semiconduc-
tor’s key file instead of creating our own.10
We had an issue with a build error containing error: '__device_dts_ord_, which was
related to the SPI3 peripheral not working correctly for the MCUBoot. After some research
we found issues online related to the same problem, where the SPI needs CONFIG_MULTI-
THREADING to be enabled, which by default is not enabled for MCUBoot. In that relation,
we found out that the MCUBoot has some separate configuration and devicetree/overlay
files stored in ncs\v2.9.0\bootloader\mcuboot\boot\zephyr\boards, where we added
our custom board files with the needed modifications for enabling multithreading and the
external flash. Afterward, we also experienced an issue with MCUBoot where the boot
image sector size was set to 128 sectors instead of the intended 512 sectors. This meant
that we did not have enough space allocated to the bootloader (MCUBoot) to be flashed
without an image. To solve this issue, we added the CONFIG_BOOT_MAX_IMG_SECTORS=512
parameter to the custom board files for the bootloader, as this was also used for the
development kits we based our board files on[145][146]. This made us able to build and
flash our prototype board.
The next major issue was that the prototype board froze during boot. Over the UART
connection, we could see that the bootloader was functional and that the firmware signa-
ture was verified. But nothing happened after the UART connection reported Booting
from the memory address of the application. The UART printout can be seen below:
Booting nRF Connect SDK v2.9.0-7787b2649840
Using Zephyr OS v3.7.99-1f8f3dc29142
Attempting to boot slot 0.
Attempting to boot from address 0x8200.
I: Trying to get Firmware version
I: Verifying signature against key 0.
I: Hash: 0x3e...f9
I: Firmware signature verified.
10In the Nordic Semiconductor Academy course - nRF Connect SDK Intermediate Lesson 9 Exercise 2
it is shown how to create and use these key files.
72
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:95==
Figure 4.39: Hello World! From the prototype board.
Firmware version 2
Booting (0x8200).
I: Firmware signature verified.
Firmware version 2
I: Setting monotonic counter (version: 2, slot: 0)
Booting (0x8200).
We found that this issue was related to the allocation of the memory addresses and the
expected size of the external flash defined in our board configuration. During our debug-
ging, we noticed that the generated partitions.yml file from our compiled firmware had a
negative flash size. After investigating, we found that our board files expected a flash of
256 Mbit or 32 MB in size, while our flash is only 128 Mbit or 16 MB in size. We resolved
the issue by adding a partition file with the correct flash size to the board hardware files11.
After setting the correct flash size, we successfully compiled, flashed, and booted a simple
Hello World program.
Although the application booted, it initially reported an error
related to SFDP and flash size, as shown in Figure 4.39. We suspect that the manually
calculated and entered parameters for the SFDP were incorrect. Once we disabled the
option of SFDP from devicetree, meaning the parameters are retrieved during runtime, the
error disappeared. Further functional testing and validation of the various subsystems—
including GNSS, Wi-Fi, and cellular connectivity—are described in Section 5.1.
4.7.4
Final Prototype Code
Using example code from Nordic Semiconductor’s lessons for different tracking types as
a foundation, we developed a ccustom firmware application for our final prototype. A
simplified overview of the code structure is illustrated in Figure 4.40. Upon boot, the code
will the system initializes all required components and establishes a connection to a CoAP
server to retrieve initial settings. The firmware application supports three configurable
parameter: tracking method, update interval, and power mode.
11partitions of flash are configured in the <board_name >_nrf9151_pm_static.yml file.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications

==PAGE:96==

Figure 4.40: Simplified flow chart of final prototype application.
74
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:97==
The tracking method can be set to SCELL (cellular), Wi-Fi, or GNSS12 and therefore
determines which type of positioning method to use. If GNSS positioning fails (e.g., due
to lack of satellite coverage), the device automatically falls back to SCELL after 5 minutes.
For our Wi-Fi positioning, it scans for five access points without RSSI values.
The update interval, defined in minutes, determines how long the device should wait
between positioning updates, e.g., how long the device sleeps or hibernates for.
The power mode can be set to Sleep or Hibernate. In Sleep mode, the nRF9151 remains
in a low-power sleep state between updates. In Hibernate mode, the nPM1300 powers
down the nRF9151 entirely to minimize energy consumption. If the PMIC detects that
the device is charging, it will override the hibernation command and place the nRF9151
into Sleep mode instead.
Once the device has acquired the data from the chosen tracking method, it will create a
payload package with the positioning data as well as a battery percentage, battery voltage,
and charging state. When the payload is sent, the device also checks for new settings to
prepare for the next cycle. When the device has obtained both fix and uploaded data,
it will manually turn off the LTE-M/NB-IoT modem and enter hibernate or sleep mode.
The manually switching of the LTE-M/NB-IoT modem on and off, provides a low-power
solution for communication regardless of whether your SIM card and/or network provider
supports power-saving techniques (PSM and eDRX).
4.8
Visualization and User Interface
Figure 4.41: Main page for visualization and user interface, showing a map of position
fixes, latest device status, and device settings.
To handle data visualization and remote interaction with the device, a system was set
up using a CoAP server provided by californium.eclipseprojects.io and Flask (Python) for
API handling and frontend rendering, Folium for map visualization, and an SQL database
to store measurement data. A CoAP client, written in Python, handles receiving and
sending data to and from the CoAP server. The architecture is based on an open-source
12For testing purposes, an ”All” method was also added to the code. This method uses all three posi-
tioning methods.

==PAGE:98==
web app provided by Nordic Semiconductor and modified extensively to fit the use case
of this project. The source code was written in Node.js, but was rewritten in Python.
4.8.1
Implementation
Initially, we created our own Python-based CoAP server, but opted instead to use the
public californium.eclipseprojects.io server to avoid the need for static IP hosting
and simplify testing.
In addition to this, we created a Flask web server13[147]. The Flask web server handles
rendering the webpage frontend to display data from the database, sending new settings
to the device, as well as changing the target CoAP server.
A simple overview of the
Flask server functionality can be seen in Figure 4.43. The Flask web server has four page
routes; the main one is the standard index of the website, a map view for the full-screen
map, an internally used events page, which is used for the map and index HTML pages
to display real-time toasts, i.e., events, and a settings page. The web server also supports
GET/POST events, such as settings, refresh, refresh, as well as database management and
export.
(a) Settings panel for device con-
figuration via CoAP.
(b) Notification panel indicating
data updates and tracker events.
Figure 4.42: Interface elements for device interaction and feedback.
These are functions from the Python code that are able to be executed by the HTML code.
The main web page is shown in Figure 4.41, while a close-up of the device settings control
panel and notification panel is shown in Figure 4.42a and Figure 4.42b, respectively. The
user is able to configure the device through the control panel, choosing the tracking method
and interval, and power mode. The user will also be able to see the latest entry from the
device on the main page, showing position, accuracy, update time, device ID, and battery
status.
13Flask is a lightweight WSGI (Web Server Gateway Interface) web application framework. Both authors
of this thesis have used Flask in previous courses as well as at our current employment.

Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:99==
Start Flask Web Server
Page Routes
