Figure 4.43: Frontend: Flask routes & handlers
The Flask page pulls events from a SQLite database that is being created by the backend.
The backend is the CoAP client application, written in Python, that does the initialization
of the database and runs the CoAP client thread. This thread will poll the target CoAP
server, either using the observe method to watch for changes on the CoAP target or
scheduled fetching of CoAP data. A simplified overview of the backend can be seen in
Figure 4.44. When data has been fetched, it is being parsed and processed for g (GNSS), s
(SCELL), or w (Wi-Fi) location information, as well as battery and charging information.
The GNSS information is received as coordinates, accuracy, as well as the GPS time from
the received data. This information is directly inserted into the database. For the cellular
(SCELL) data, information on the MCC, MNC, area code, and Cell ID is sent to Unwired
Labs for processing using their API, and a location and accuracy are returned to be
inserted into the database. For a description of cellular positioning, see Section 3.6. The
Wi-Fi positioning works identically to the cellular data, except that in this case, it is a list
of BSSIDs that are being sent to Google’s Geolocation API, and a location and accuracy
are returned to be inserted into the database. For a description of Wi-Fi positioning, see
Section 3.5.
4.9
Summary
This chapter details the end-to-end process of designing and fabricating our ultra-low-
power tracking device, covering system architecture, component selection, PCB design,
and user interface development. We began by outlining the overall system architecture,
with integration of GNSS, LTE-M/NB-IoT communication, Wi-Fi positioning, and power
management, and presenting the chosen components for the project.
We highlighted
the design and layout process, involving multi-layer stackup planning and antenna/RF
implementation, while also exploring wireless charging integration, where custom coils were
designed and tested. We have presented our assembled PCB prototype, complemented
by designing two 3D-printed enclosure variants to fit both wireless and magnetic pogo
charging versions. On the software side, we have developed with the nRF Connect SDK
to implement device firmware for ultra-low power geolocation tracking. A backend and user
interface are integrated for visualization, remote configuration, and data management.

5
Testing and Evaluation
Measurement and validation have played a central role throughout the development of this
project. Early-stage testing on development kits from Nordic Semiconductor allowed us to
evaluate baseline performance and feasibility of key technologies such as GNSS, LTE-M,
NB-IoT, and Wi-Fi, while also helping to identify hardware and firmware features required
to support ultra-low power operation. These early insights were instrumental in shaping
our system architecture, influencing decisions around our main ICs, power management,
and firmware development.
During and after the hardware design process, additional
measurements were carried out to validate the custom PCB, verify the correct integration
of critical subsystems, and demonstrate the system’s overall functionality.
Due to time constraints and the iterative nature of hardware development, the focus of
this chapter is to present the relevant testing and a set of proof-of-concept measurements
that demonstrate correct operation of the most critical components for our custom board,
including the positioning modules, wireless communication interfaces, power management
IC, and Qi-based wireless charging system. The results should confirm that the board
operates as intended and provide a foundation for future iterations, more in-depth testing,
and further firmware development.
5.1
Initial Testing and Verification of Subsystems
After resolving the compile and bootloader initialization issues as explained in Section 4.7,
we proceeded to validate the individual hardware components on our custom PCB. To be-
gin, we verified the GPIO functionality by running a simple LED test application. This
script cycled through red, green, and blue channels on the onboard RGB LED connected
to the nRF9151. The test was successful, confirming that the SiP and basic pin routing
on the board functioned as expected. We then developed a series of test applications tar-
geting individual subsystems to verify correct electrical design, connectivity, and expected
performance.
5.1.1
Wi-Fi
To verify the correct operation of the NN02-101 antenna booster, nRF7000 IC, diplexer,
matching network, and transmission lines for the Wi-Fi subsystem, we used and modified
the Wi-Fi: Scan sample from the nRF Connect SDK. This application enables power to
the nRF7000 through our LDO and power switch, and initiates Wi-Fi scan operations in
the 2.4 and 5 GHz bands, depending on the type of scan profile defined. It then outputs
the SSID and Received Signal Strength Indicator(RSSI) values for the detected access
point over UART.
In our first test, we used the default scanning profile, which performs both active and
passive scanning for 2.4 and 5 GHz access points. The test was conducted at our workplace,
where we registered 48 access points from 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz channels, and where the
output of the first ten access points is seen in the first column of Figure 5.1. We can
see that the signal strength of the first 10 entries ranged from -53 dBm to -60 dBm, well
within the “very good” signal range (typically above -67 dBm)[148]. This must indicate
suﬀicient transmission line and matching circuit performance, as well as proper operation
of the ICs and components for the Wi-Fi subsystem.
For further validation and comparison of our results, we flashed the same firmware onto
the Nordic Thingy:91 X development kit and placed it next to our custom board. Here

our custom board as seen in the third column of Figure 5.1.

nearly identical RSSI values, confirming that our 5 GHz support was also fully functional.
The results of the two boards are shown in Figure 5.2.
5.1.2
GNSS
To evaluate the functionality of our GNSS subsystem, including the matching network,
antenna booster element (NN03-320), and the LNA (SKY65943-11), we used one of the
applications we developed while taking online courses at Nordic Developer Academy. The
application activates the GNSS receiver and logs satellite information by printing the
satellites that the GNSS receiver is currently tracking and their signal strength as the
carrier-to-noise density ratio (C/N0) through UART. The signal strength from that satel-
lite has to be above 30 dB/Hz (cn0=300) for the GNSS receiver to consider the satellite
“healthy” and use it in calculations for location tracking[149].

The nRF9151’s integrated GNSS receiver was configured to start without any assistance
data. During testing in an outdoor environment with moderate sky visibility, the system
successfully acquired multiple satellites and logged their carrier-to-noise density ratio. A
sample from the output is shown in Figure 5.3. The system found six satellites during
the search phase, with all six of the satellites’ signal strengths being above the needed 30
dB/Hz. This indicates that the antenna booster element, matching network, transmission
lines, LNA and GNSS receiver is working as intended.
Just as for the Wi-Fi verification, we flashed the same GNSS firmware application to
the Nordic Thingy:91 X. By running these boards side-by-side, we were able to compare
satellite acquisition behavior and signal strength. The results across both platform were
consistent, confirming that our hardware, particularly the antenna, layout, and matching
network, is functional and performs on par with Nordics products. A sample from the
Thingy:91 X is also depicted in Figure 5.3.
5.1.3
LTE-M and NB-IoT
To validate the LTE connectivity subsystem of our custom PCB, we first developed a test
application that initializes the modem and establishes a connection with the LTE network.
During this process, the application logs the AT commands used throughout the modem
initialization and network registration, allowing us to monitor each step and ensure that
the modem behaves as expected. The UART debug output is shown below:
Booting nRF Connect SDK v2.9.0-7787b2649840
Using Zephyr OS v3.7.99-1f8f3dc29142
<inf> LTE: Initializing modem library
<inf> LTE: Connecting to LTE network
+CEREG: 2,"5DE1","02763716",7
+CSCON: 1
<inf> LTE: RRC mode: Connected
+CEREG: 5,"5DE1","02763716",7,,,"11100000","11100000"
<inf> LTE: Network registration status: Connected - roaming
<inf> LTE: Connected to LTE network
+CSCON: 0
<inf> LTE: RRC mode: Idle
The output confirms that the modem is successfully initialized and able to connect to the
LTE network. From the output above, the fourth field of the +CEREG response being ”7”
indicates connection via LTE-M. A similar test was performed while forcing NB-IoT, where
the fourth field being ”9” indicates NB-IoT connectivity. These tests confirmed that the
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
81
==PAGE:104==
Figure 5.4: Validation from PC of PUT and GET requests.
modem, SIM card interface, and RF parts for LTE-M and NB-IoT were operating correctly.
In addition to this, we implemented a second test application that established a bidirec-
tional communication between our board and another CoAP client through a CoAP server,
allowing us to test a proof-of-concept for remote interaction with the board from a PC. The
application was connected to the public CoAP test server californium.eclipseprojects.io,
hosted by the Eclipse Californium project. The firmware sets up our custom board to
send a GET request to the resource on the server when Button 1 is pressed on our board,
and prints the response payload via UART. If the button is pressed again, it sends a PUT
request to the resource, transmitting a user-defined payload (”Hi from MINT!” in our
case) from the board to the server, which then can be displayed on the PC.
The output of the UART on the board shows that the board connect to the LTE network,
get an IP address, connects to the server, able to send a GET request and a PUT request.
The output is shown below:
...

==PAGE:105==
This test served as a complete end-to-end validation of the LTE connectivity, IP stack
functionality, and the application-layer communication required for the core operation of
the tracker device.
5.1.4
Fuel Gauge and Charging Validation
To evaluate the battery monitoring and charging system of our custom PCB, we conducted
testing of the integrated fuel gauge functionality within the nPM1300 PMIC. The objective
was to verify whether the fuel gauge could correctly report parameters such as battery
voltage, current, temperature, and state-of-charge.
Using a dedicated test application
based on Nordic’s Power Management and Fuel Gauge APIs, we logged real-time data
over UART. A sample output of the fuel gauge is shown below:
<inf> fuel_gauge: V: 4.072, I: 0.032, T: 23.89, SoC: 91.73
<inf> fuel_gauge: Fuel gauge entered idle mode
These logs were captured at 10-second intervals over a few minutes and showed stable
values in terms of voltage (￿4.07 V), current draw (￿32 µA), and temperature (￿24 °C).
The state-of-charge value remained consistent at approximately 91%, confirming that the
battery level is being tracked over time. The logs also showed the system entering fuel
gauge idle mode, which is a low-power monitoring state indicating that the PMIC correctly
minimizes current draw when no active battery events are ongoing.
In addition to fuel gauging, we validated the charging functionality using our magnetic
pogo pin charger. When the device was connected with the charger, the nPM1300 correctly
initiated the charging process. Visual confirmation was provided via the PMIC status
LED, which illuminated to indicate charging. The Fuel gauge application also confirmed
the charging, with the state-of-charge increasing over time. This confirmed that the pogo
charger, charge controller, and battery charging logic are working as intended.
5.1.5
Sensors - BME688 and ADXL367
Due to time constraints, we were not able to fully test and integrate the BME688 envi-
ronmental sensor and the ADXL367 ultra-low power accelerometer into our final system.
Initial attempts were made to interface with the BME688 via I2C, but communication is-
sues prevented successful data retrieval. The root cause of this problem remains unknown
and requires further debugging, such as validating the I2C bus configuration, checking
hardware connections, and analyzing signal integrity. The ADXL367 was included in the
hardware design, but has not yet been brought up or tested. Future work will involve
bringing up both sensors, evaluating their power consumption, and integrating them into
the tracker application firmware.
5.2
Power Consumption Measurements
One of the primary objectives of this project is ultra-low power operation. To evaluate the
power consumption of our board and the different tracking methods, we used the firmware
application described in Section 4.7.4. The application made it easy to switch between the
different tracking methods and between hibernation and regular sleep mode for testing. To
capture the power data, we hooked up our custom board with Nordic’s Power Profiler Kit
II (PPK2), which allowed us to measure the current consumption during startup, while
obtaining location fix, data transmission, and idle phases. In the following, we will focus
solely on the power consumption of each tracking method, while Section 5.3 will focus on
the accuracy of each method and compare, and Section 5.4 will compare the methods in
a real-world scenario.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
83
==PAGE:106==
5.2.1
GNSS, Wi-Fi and Cellular
The three subfigures in Figure 5.5 show example power traces from a single fix attempt for
each of the three positioning methods using regular sleep mode. As seen from Figure 5.5a,
GNSS requires the highest current and longest time to obtain a fix and upload the location
to our backend, where the example consumes an average of 46.46 mA over 28.12 s, resulting
in a total charge consumption of 1.31 C. The GNSS was tested under open-sky and hot
start conditions. The current trace can be broken down as follows:
1. Wake-up and system initialization (brief current spikes),
2. Continuous GNSS activity (flat plateau at approx. 60 mA),
3. Fix confirmation and processing (short dip)
4. LTE-M connection and data transmission
5. Enter low-power mode.
Wi-Fi scanning shows a moderate current draw, as depicted in Figure 5.5b, where the
example has a current draw of 34.80 mA and a shorter fix time of 15.66 s, with a corre-
sponding charge consumption of 0.55 C. The Wi-Fi scan is scanning for 5 access points
and uploading these to the backend for location fix, without RSSI values. The current
trace in the example shows:
1. Wake-up and system initialization (brief current spikes),
2. Wi-Fi scanning (flat around 60 mA with short burst above 200 mA)
3. Processing scan (relatively flat and low consumption)
4. LTE-M connection and data transmission
5. Enter low-power mode.
Cellular positioning proved to be the most energy-eﬀicient option in our test, as seen in
Figure 5.5c, where the example only required 7.365 s at an average current of 33.78 mA,
consuming 248.78 mC in total. The power profile shown in the example is structured as:
1. Wake-up and system initialization (small current spikes)
2. Registration process with the network service provider (current consumption in-
creases to ￿40 mA).
3. Data transmission of position information(burst of spikes above 100 mA).
4. Enter low-power mode.
These results demonstrate how the power demand varies a lot between the different track-
ing technologies. An average power consumption of more samples is shown in Table 5.1,
also showing both sleep modes, which will be compared in the following section.
5.2.2
Sleep vs. Hibernation
As mentioned earlier, the nPM1300 offers to put the system into hibernation mode, where
most components are powered down, and wake-up requires a full restart, but offers a signif-
icantly lower standby current compared to sleep mode. In sleep mode, the system retains
RAM and context, allowing faster wake-up but consuming more standby current. We
tested out the three different tracking technologies to compare the consumption between
sleep and hibernation mode.

Table 5.1: Average current, time, and total charge consumption for each tracking mode in
both sleep and hibernation.
Figure 5.6 shows the current consumption measured with the PPK2 during idle periods
for both modes. In sleep mode for all tracking methods, the board maintained an average
consumption of 12.30 µA, whereas hibernation mode reduced the current draw drastically
to just 0.60 µA, demonstrating the effectiveness of hibernation mode.
Table 5.1 summarizes the average current, duration, and resulting charge consumption
for each tracking mode under both power configurations. Across all methods, hibernation
consistently reduced idle current but came at the cost of longer boot-up and total opera-
tion time. This is particularly noticeable for GNSS, where hibernation nearly tripled the
duration, increasing the charge used during active despite the lower idle current1.
During the hibernation-based tests, we observed several cases where the device experienced
delayed or failed network reattachments.
These issues were most noticeable when the
device woke up after hibernation and reentered the network within a short interval (e.g.,
20-30 seconds). We suspect this may be due to the full modem reinitialization that occurs
after hibernation. In some cases, the device may have been temporarily ”greylisted” by the
network due to frequent reattach attempts in a short timeframe. This could indicate that
the mode must be carefully managed in applications, especially with short hibernation
intervals.
These findings highlight an important trade-off: while hibernation minimizes standby
power consumption, the increased wake-up overhead can outweigh the benefits for frequent
tracking events, making the optimal power mode dependent on use case and expected
tracking interval.
5.2.3
Battery Lifetime
To evaluate the real-world viability of our tracker in ultra-low power applications, we con-
ducted battery lifetime estimations based on the measured average current consumption
across different tracking modes and sleep modes. The estimations are based on ideal condi-
tions for the battery, not accounting for self-discharge, temperature, or battery condition
over time, and the power measurement results from Table 5.1.
The estimated battery lifetime is calculated based on the 680 mAh Li-Po battery used
for our prototype, while also having a 20% discharge safety margin to avoid significant
damage and reduced lifespan of the battery. The total average daily energy consumption
is the sum of energy used during active and sleep periods. The battery lifetime in days,
accounting for the safety margin, is:
1For the hibernation test, it should be noted that the application connects to our backend to obtain user
settings before each tracking attempt, due to the system being powered down. This is also a contributing
factor for the longer event durations.

N tactive Iactive + (T −N tactive) Isleep

(5.1)
where Cbat is the battery capacity in mAh, η = 0.8 is the usable battery fraction accounting

duration of one active event in seconds, Iactive is the current during active mode in mA,
Isleep is the current during sleep mode in mA, and T = 86400 s is the total number of
seconds in one day.
The results are shown in Table 5.2 and Table 5.3. These results highlight the trade-offs
between sleep and hibernation modes across the different tracking technologies. In low-
frequency tracking scenarios (e.g., once per day), hibernation offers the longest battery
life—up to 2649 days (over 7 years) with cellular tracking. However, in high-frequency
scenarios (e.g., 24 daily events), sleep mode can outperform hibernation due to its lower
wake-up and reinitialization overhead. While even for GNSS, the once per day tracking
scenario, the sleep mode outperformed the hibernation mode. Overall, these estimations
confirms that our system is capable of multi-year battery life in realistic low-power appli-
cations and multi-month lifetimes even under frequent tracking conditions2.
5.3
Positioning Accuracy
To evaluate the practical accuracy of the supported positioning technologies, we conducted
comparative measurements of GNSS, Wi-Fi, and cellular-based location fixes across mul-
tiple real-world environments. The purpose of these tests was to understand how each

their absolute positioning error relative to a known ground truth.
Each tracking method was activated individually to retrieve a position fix. The coordinates
obtained from GNSS, Wi-Fi, and cellular-based positioning were collected and plotted rel-
ative to the ground truth. Example of 5 fixes for each technology is depicted in Figure 5.7,
Figure 5.8, and Figure 5.9. As seen from the figures, GNSS gives an average positioning
error of ￿4.8 m, being very accurate. Wi-Fi gives an average error of 34.8 m, less accurate
compared to GNSS, but still quite good, and as our test application is only uploading 5
access points, increased accuracy could maybe be obtained if more access points or RSSI
values were added to the payload. For cellular, the average error was 534.7 m, and was
very dependent on which cell tower the device was connected to, as it could show accuracy
of 100 m to 3 km, but better accuracy could maybe be achieved by using information from
nearby cell towers (MCELL).
5.4
Field Testing
To evaluate the real-world performance of our tracking system, we conducted a field test
across multiple public transport modes in the Greater Copenhagen area. The objective
was to assess how well GNSS, Wi-Fi, and cellular-based positioning performed in dynamic
urban conditions, including both indoor (e.g., metro and trains) and outdoor environ-
ments. As shown in Figure 5.10, the route began at Bistrup Trafikplads and concluded at
Gammel Strand in central Copenhagen. GNSS position fixes are shown in green, Wi-Fi
in blue, cellular in red, and the reference route (recorded using a phone running Strava) is
shown in purple. Zoomed-in pictures of the route can be seen in Appendix D. It is impor-
tant to note that the points shown on the map represent the raw location fixes reported
by each tracking method (GNSS, Wi-Fi, and cellular). These points indicate where the
90
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:113==
Figure 5.10: Overview of the entire test route from Bistrup Trafikplads to Gammel Strand,
showing GNSS (green), Wi-Fi (blue), Cellular (red) position fixes across public transport
modes, and true route (purple).
system believes it is based on the specific positioning method, not the estimated accuracy
or uncertainty radius associated with those fixes. Therefore, the map visualizes the spatial
output of each technology, not their confidence levels or error margins. The device was
set up to upload location data every 5 minutes.
This test allowed us to compare accuracy, coverage, and reliability of the three track-
ing technologies under realistic usage scenarios involving bus rides, S-train travel, and
metro transit. As seen from Figure 5.10 and Appendix D, GNSS generally provided very
good positional accuracy, closely following the actual route, even during bus and train
segments. However, as expected, GNSS performance disappeared in the metro, where
satellite visibility was lost entirely.
Wi-Fi-based positioning also demonstrated good accuracy. However, it proved less reliable
at higher speeds, such as during S-train travel, and was ineffective in the metro due to the
limited presence of Wi-Fi networks.
Cellular positioning showed the most mixed results. Although often less accurate than
GNSS or Wi-Fi, it occasionally produced surprisingly accurate fixes; this could be due to
the cellular infrastructure along the train tracks. Notably, cellular was the only technology
that maintained a position fix in the metro (between Østerport Station and Gammel
Strand), where infrastructure is installed to support mobile connectivity underground.
Overall, these results emphasize how the different positioning methods can complement
each other and support the case for a hybrid geolocation device that can dynamically
leverage the most suitable technology based on the environment.

A small, no-name Qi receiver module was used to test the prototype coils and confirm
that they worked as intended. This module had a small wire-wound receiver coil that we
also measured and tested to use as a comparison.
To measure the inductance of the coils, we used a Hewlett-Packard 4194A Impedance/Gain-
Phase Analyzer, depicted in Figure 5.12, kindly lent to us by Cobham Satcom. The free
space inductance was measured Ls as seen in Figure 5.11b and for the L′
s was measured in
a setup closely resembling the final configuration, using the intended battery, a 3D printed
element, and a ferrite layer as seen in Figure 5.11a. An image of the screen on the device
can be seen in Figure 5.12. The results of the simulated and measured DC resistance and
inductance are shown in Table 5.5 and Table 5.4 respectively.
All five coils were then connected to the no-name Qi receiver PCB with two 0.47 Ωresistors
in series with four 27 Ωload resistors in parallel; this resistor configuration resulted in
a 7.70 Ωload. The Qi receiver PCB should be rated for 5 V 1A according to the item
listing, but testing with a resistance near 5 Ωresulted in a voltage drop to around 3 V.
Since the charging current for this project does not need to be more than 300-500 mA,
but the supply voltage should be 4 V minimum, we opted to use the 7.70Ωload for our
testing as this resulted in approximately a 500 mA current draw on the system with at
least 4 V.
The current supplied by the receiver PCB was measured using the PPK2 from Nordic
Semiconductor, and the voltage was measured using an oscilloscope. An overview of the
test setup can be seen in Figure 5.13.
The measured results showed that the PCB used for testing the coils most likely would
not deliver 5 V and 1 A, but drawing 1 A would result in a voltage drop below 4 V. The
measurements also showed a tie between Design 3, Design 4, and the wire-wound coil that
the PCB arrived with. These preliminary measurements did show that for our purpose, a
PCB coil would be suﬀicient for power delivery.
5.6
Qi Receiver
As part of our design, we aimed to implement wireless charging using a custom Qi re-
ceiver circuit. However, during testing, the receiver failed to operate as expected. When
placed on a Qi-compatible transmitter, the coil would heat up, and a low voltage could be
measured on the board, but the charging circuit did not activate or deliver current to the
battery. Multiple debugging steps were attempted, including resoldering the Qi receiver
IC, recalculating, and replacing various passive components, but the board would still not
activate.
To verify that our coil design was functional, we soldered one of our test coils onto the no-
name Qi receiver module, which was also used for the initial coil measurement presented
in Section 5.5. This combination successfully worked with the same transmitter and was

5.7
Summary
This chapter has presented the validation and performance testing of our custom geolo-
cation tracker, covering critical subsystems like LTE-M/NB-IoT communication, Wi-Fi
scanning, GNSS, power management, and wireless charging. Through a combination of
proof-of-concept tests, real-world measurements, and direct comparison with Nordic Semi-
conductor’s development kits, we confirmed that most of our custom hardware is working
as intended.
Power profiling confirmed that GNSS is the most power-hungry tracking method, whereas
cellular positioning is the most energy-eﬀicient. Furthermore, hibernation mode signifi-
cantly reduces standby power compared to sleep mode, but comes with trade-offs in boot
time and network reattachment reliability.
Battery lifetime estimations showed that the device can achieve multi-year operation in
low-frequency tracking scenarios (up to 2649 days with hibernation and 1402 days with
sleep with one update per day) and multi-month lifetime in frequent tracking use cases (up
to 118 days with hibernation and 215 days with sleep with one update per hour). Field
testing across public transportation environments highlighted the strengths and limita-
tions of each positioning method, emphasizing the value of a hybrid approach. Finally,
preliminary Qi coil tests demonstrated suﬀicient power delivery capability from custom-
designed PCB coils. However, the custom Qi receiver proved unsuccessful and was unable
to deliver power.

6
Discussion
This chapter reflects on the key technical decisions, trade-offs, and learning outcomes that
shaped the outcome of our project. Rather than just listing what we built, we take a
step back to evaluate how and why we made certain choices.
We begin by reviewing
how well our final prototype met the system requirements we defined in the beginning
of this project, highlighting both the successes and the areas left for future work. Other
sections focus on our motivation for designing a custom PCB from scratch, and how our
limited prior experience in complex hardware design shaped the project, both in terms of
challenges and steep learning curves. Finally, we reflect on lessons learned, prototyping
strategies, and possible improvements to our workflow if we were to tackle a similar project
again in the future.
6.1
Review of Requirements
An overview of our requirement is given in Table 6.1. Holding our project requirements
up against our tracker solution, we have fulfilled 8 out of the 8 core system requirements
defined at the beginning of the project, all were successfully implemented and validated
through measurements and field testing. Regarding the five optional features, one was par-
tially implemented: although our custom Qi receiver design did not function as intended,
we achieved wireless charging functionality using an off-the-shelf Qi receiver module. The
remaining optional features, FOTA and Assisted or Predicted GNSS, were not imple-
mented due to time limitations. Overall, we fulfilled all core requirements and achieved
partial success with the optional features, while also demonstrating a functional prototype.
6.2
Areas for Future Development and Test
As the project progressed, we identified several areas of interest that could not be explored
in depth within the project’s timeframe.
As future work, we think it would be very
interesting to test the eﬀiciency both in power and functionality of NB-IoT compared to
LTE-M, as well as assisted GNSS tracking methods for even faster positioning. It would
be a nice addition to the functionality of our prototype to have the BME688 environment
sensor working, so information on the environment the device is in would be sent to the
server as well. Also, it would be a great addition to have the ability to use the low-power
accelerometer to send an interrupt to the nPM1300 PMIC to wake up the device from
hibernation upon movement, and otherwise having the device in hibernation mode when
stationary. This would have allowed the nRF9151 to be powered down when stationary
and only have the PMIC and accelerometer drawing power.
Another area that needs deeper investigation is the Qi wireless charging functionality. We
were able to include a Qi receiver in the design with our custom PCB coils, however our
Qi receiver board did not work. The issue remains unresolved, and further debugging is
needed to determine where the problem is.
From both a security and functionality standpoint, it would be a fun addition to host
a private CoAP server and use encryption for the communication. In addition to this,
polishing up the backend and front-end and adding login, device registration, and support
for multiple devices would be an ideal implementation.
A deep dive into the functionality of Wi-Fi positioning would be an interesting topic as
well. Testing the most eﬀicient number of BSSIDs to get a valid position and testing

but within low tolerance.
Connectivity
Supports LTE-M and NB-IoT (LPWAN) for long-
range, low-power communication.
Coverage
Tested on national networks with reliable connectivity.
While LTE-M and NB-IoT should give us coverage
over most of Europe.
GNSS positioning
Accurate positioning within 20 meters in open-sky
conditions.
Wi-Fi positioning (2.4 GHz)
Wi-Fi scanning and positioning have been imple-
mented for both 2.4 GHz and 5 GHz.
Power management
Rechargeable Li-Po battery with power management
IC, being able to charge wirelessly or using magnetic
pogo pins.
Data platform
A web dashboard and backend have been created for
location data visualization and database for data stor-
age.
Optional Features
Status and Remarks
Wireless charging
Our custom Qi receiver was not successful, but our
PCB coils showed promising results and integrated
with a no-name off-the-shelf Qi receiver.
Downlink configuration
Remote configuration of parameters is supported by
using the web dashboard, enabling the choice of track-
ing method, tracking frequency, and sleep mode.
FOTA support
Left as future work.
A-GNSS / P-GNSS
Potential for future power optimization, but not im-
plemented yet.
Enclosure
Two 3D-printed cases are designed to match the form
factor, one for the wireless charging model and one for
the magnetic pogo charging model.
Table 6.1: Review of core and optional system requirements. Requirement cells are color-
coded: Implemented, Partly implemented, Not implemented.
98
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:121==
for different scan modes, such as passive scanning, active scanning, and non-overlapping
channels, to maximize the eﬀiciency of Wi-Fi positioning. We could also test if adding the
RSSI values for the access points could improve accuracy.
Another area of future work is MCELL (multicell cellular positioning). While our proto-
type used SCELL (single-cell) positioning, incorporating MCELL, which uses information
from multiple cell towers, could improve accuracy significantly while maintaining low
power consumption.
We also discussed developing a ”light” version of the device. In this version, the Wi-Fi
module, sensors, and SIM card holder (implement softSIM) could be removed entirely
to reduce both cost and board space, allowing for even more compact design.
Given
the strong battery lifetime we achieved during testing, we even considered powering the
light version with a coin cell battery. This would allow us to make the device even more
compact.
6.3
Motivation for Custom PCB Design
One of the core goals of this project was to approach engineering as more than just
theoretical or modular experimentation, we wanted to take an idea or challenge and carry
it through the full development process, from concept to a complete, working product.
Our goal was to simulate a professional product development flow, where constraints such
as size, power, cost, and manufacturability all have to be balanced.
Our motivation came partly from a lack of courses offered by DTU. While DTU offers
many strong courses in embedded systems, signal processing, and communication theory,
there are very few opportunities to learn PCB design in a structured, hands-on way. Most
courses rely on prefabricated evaluation boards, simulation environments, or breadboard
assembly, so we saw this thesis as an opportunity to learn something new and directly
applicable to real-world engineering.
Before this project, our experience with PCB design was limited to a few simple hobby
projects, mostly involving simple 2-layer designs with only a handful of components on
them. Jumping into a complex multi-layer board like this project was a huge leap. This
limited prior experience had a dual effect on our thesis work. On one hand, it made the
initial hardware development phase more challenging and time-consuming. We had to
invest significant effort in learning advanced PCB design practices while simultaneously
building the actual system. Many iterations were needed to get the antenna matching
networks, power rails, and layout constraints working together without introducing new
issues. Reading datasheets, application notes, design guides, and reference layouts became
a core part of our workflow. On the other hand, this steep learning curve also made the
thesis more rewarding. By building a system of this complexity, we were pushed beyond
the typical boundaries of student projects. We transitioned from simply wiring up modules
to thinking like system designers.
6.4
Lessons Learned
This thesis project has been one of the most challenging and rewarding experiences of our
academic studies. One of the most important lessons we have learned is that PCB design
is more than just placing components and routing traces. As soon as real constraints and
requirements are introduced, such as RF integration, power integrity, manufacturability,
and stackup planning, the process becomes exponentially more complex. A layout that
looks clean and functional on screen can easily result in failure once built, due to things
like mismatched impedance, parasitic capacitance, or noise/crosstalk.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
99
==PAGE:122==
Another key takeaway was the importance of planning for iteration. We initially hoped
to get as close as possible to a final design in the first revision, but quickly realized
that hardware development is an iterative process. There are always things you cannot
fully predict, such as component substitutions or tolerances from the final manufacturer.
In the future, we would approach early development by designing a simpler prototype
version first, focusing on validating the most critical subsystems (e.g., power, MCU, RF
paths), before committing to a highly integrated, space-constrained final layout. That
said, splitting the development into two or three board revisions would have been diﬀicult
to manage within the time constraints of a thesis project, especially given our limited prior
experience with complex PCB design. Therefore, choosing to target a complete board in a
single revision was a calculated risk that aligned with our learning goals and the available
timeline.
One trade-off we encountered was the use of dual-sided component placement. It helped
reduce board size and improve routing, but it made prototyping more diﬀicult. Manual
rework and testing became slower, and it increased the cost and complexity of manufac-
turing. Looking back, we would likely stick to a single-sided layout in early prototypes,
even if it meant a larger board.
6.5
Summary
This project pushed us far beyond simple academic exercises. We not only delivered a
prototype that met all of our core requirements, but also gained real-world experience in
system integration, RF design, power optimization, and embedded development. While
some features like FOTA, A-GNSS, and full wireless charging remain unfinished, we laid
the groundwork for those in future revisions. We also discussed the potential of building a
“light” version of the tracker for even smaller applications, potentially powered by a coin
cell and without Wi-Fi or sensor components. Ultimately, this project gave us an end-to-
end experience in product development. We leave this thesis with a stronger engineering
mindset, deeper technical skills, and a better understanding of what it takes to go from
an idea to a robust, functioning product.
100 Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications
==PAGE:123==
7
Conclusion
This thesis has presented the design, development, and evaluation of a compact, ultra-low
power geolocation tracking device capable of operating across a range of environments
and use cases. We set out to address the limitations of traditional GNSS-based solutions
by integrating hybrid positioning technologies—GNSS, cellular, and Wi-Fi—into a single,
battery-powered device. The project set out to balance accuracy, power consumption, and
connectivity within a credit card–sized form factor.
Through a systematic approach, we explored relevant technologies, evaluated design trade-
offs, and implemented a complete hardware and software solution based on the Nordic
nRF9151 SiP, nRF7000, and nPM1300 ICs.
We built a custom PCB with integrated
antennas, a Li-Po battery, and support for both magnetic pogo-pin and wireless charging.
On the software side, we developed embedded firmware, cloud integration, and a web
dashboard for real-time monitoring and remote configuration.
A key part of this thesis was defining measurable system requirements from the outset. As
shown in the requirement review, all eight core requirements were fully met and validated
through measurements and field tests.
These include long battery life (exceeding 180
days), reliable GNSS and Wi-Fi positioning, LPWAN communication (LTE-M/NB-IoT),
and an integrated data platform. Among five optional features, one—wireless charging—
was partially realized through the use of an off-the-shelf Qi receiver module, while others,
like FOTA and A-GNSS, remain as opportunities for future development.
Field testing revealed the benefits and limitations of each positioning method.
GNSS
provided high accuracy but consumed significant power, while Wi-Fi and cellular methods
offered energy-eﬀicient alternatives with reduced precision. Power profiling and battery
lifetime estimations demonstrated that our tracker could operate for several months to
years, depending on the update frequency and power mode, validating the viability of our
design for real-world deployment. These results also highlight the importance of adaptable
tracking solutions that can switch between positioning methods based on the environment
or application requirements.
This project also offered us valuable hands-on experience in end-to-end product develop-
ment. From antenna layout and power optimization to embedded firmware and backend
infrastructure, we encountered and solved real-world engineering challenges across mul-
tiple domains. The process has not only strengthened our technical foundation but also
deepened our understanding of designing practical and scalable embedded systems.
7.1
Future Work
Although the current prototype meets the core design objectives, several opportunities for
improvement and extension remain. These include:
• NB-IoT Eﬀiciency Analysis: Conduct a comparative study of NB-IoT and LTE-
M in terms of power consumption, network reliability, and handover performance.
• Assisted GNSS (A-GNSS/P-GNSS): Implement and evaluate assisted GNSS
methods to reduce time-to-first-fix (TTFF).
• Environmental Sensing: Debug and integrate the BME688 environmental sensor
to transmit temperature, humidity, air pressure, and gas data alongside location.
Satellite/WiFi and NB-IoT/LTE-M Tracker for Compact Ultra-Low Power Geolocation Applications 101
==PAGE:124==
• Motion-Based Wakeup: Use the low-power accelerometer to trigger wakeups of
the device from low-power mode based on movement detection.
• Qi Wireless Charging Debugging: Investigate and resolve the issues with the
custom Qi receiver implementation.
• Secure Communication and CoAP Server: Develop a secure communication
protocol with encryption and host a private CoAP server to improve both privacy
and functionality.
• Backend and Frontend Improvements: Expand the web platform to support
user authentication, device registration, multi-device management, and a more ro-
bust user experience.
• Wi-Fi Positioning Optimization: Evaluate scan parameters (e.g., active vs. pas-
sive scanning, BSSID count, non-overlapping channels) and RSSI-based improve-
ments to enhance positioning accuracy and eﬀiciency.
• MCELL Positioning: Extend cellular positioning to include MCELL (multi-cell)
for improved accuracy using data from multiple towers.
• “Light” Version Development: Design a minimal variant without Wi-Fi, sensors,
or SIM card holder (using softSIM), potentially powered by a coin cell, to enable an
even smaller and lower-cost tracker.
In conclusion, this thesis demonstrates that it is possible to combine compact hardware,
eﬀicient firmware, and hybrid positioning technologies into a single, ultra-low power track-
ing device. By meeting all core requirements, we achieved a system capable of accurate
positioning, reliable communication, and long battery life within a small form factor. The
project not only validates our technical approach but also showcases a complete and de-
ployable solution, ready to address real-world tracking needs in modern IoT applications.

==PAGE:150==
C
Solder Station Setup
Figure C.1: Setup used by Pranom Anderson from Cobham Satcom to solder some of the
critical components on our custom board.
Pranom Anderson from Cobham Satcom performed the soldering of our custom boards.
She primarily preferred soldering by hand, utilizing very thin soldering iron tips ideal for
the small components and fine-pitch pins on our boards. These thin tips, however, have
limited durability and need frequent replacement after soldering a few components. For
components that were diﬀicult to solder manually, she employed an underheat technique
followed by a heat gun.

F
Contribution Breakdown
This appendix provides an overview of the division of work between Andreas and Alexander
throughout the thesis project.
We have collaborated closely on most aspects, with tasks generally shared evenly. The
use of tools such as Altium, VS Code, Overleaf, combined with physical presence and
screen sharing over Discord, has made it possible to distribute the work quite evenly, as
we have been working together continuously. We have both contributed equally in effort
and responsibility to ensure the successful completion of the project. The approximate
contribution percentages for key project areas are as follows:
Task/Area

Alexander

Hello, here is some text without a meaning. This text should show what a printed text will look like
at this place. If you read this text, you will get no information. Really? Is there no information?
Is there a difference between this text and some nonsense like “Huardest gefburn”? Kjift – not at
all! A blind text like this gives you information about the selected font, how the letters are written
and an impression of the look. This text should contain all letters of the alphabet and it should be
written in of the original language. There is no need for special content, but the length of words
should match the language.

University of
Denmark
